        <title>Nanopore sequencing-based genome assembly and evolutionary genomics of circum-basmati rice | Genome Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="The circum-basmati group of cultivated Asian rice (Oryza sativa) contains many iconic varieties and is widespread in the Indian subcontinent. Despite its economic and cultural importance, a high-quality reference genome is currently lacking, and the group&#8217;s evolutionary history is not fully resolved. To address these gaps, we use long-read nanopore sequencing and assemble the genomes of two circum-basmati rice varieties. We generate two high-quality, chromosome-level reference genomes that represent the 12 chromosomes of Oryza. The assemblies show a contig N50 of 6.32&#8201;Mb and 10.53&#8201;Mb for Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid, respectively. Using our highly contiguous assemblies, we characterize structural variations segregating across circum-basmati genomes. We discover repeat expansions not observed in japonica&#8212;the rice group most closely related to circum-basmati&#8212;as well as the presence and absence variants of over 20&#8201;Mb, one of which is a circum-basmati-specific deletion of a gene regulating awn length. We further detect strong evidence of admixture between the circum-basmati and circum-aus groups. This gene flow has its greatest effect on chromosome 10, causing both structural variation and single-nucleotide polymorphism to deviate from genome-wide history. Lastly, population genomic analysis of 78 circum-basmati varieties shows three major geographically structured genetic groups: Bhutan/Nepal, India/Bangladesh/Myanmar, and Iran/Pakistan. The availability of high-quality reference genomes allows functional and evolutionary genomic analyses providing genome-wide evidence for gene flow between circum-aus and circum-basmati, describes the nature of circum-basmati structural variation, and reveals the presence/absence variation in this important and iconic rice variety group."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://genomebiology.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Genome Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Nanopore sequencing-based genome assembly and evolutionary genomics of circum-basmati rice"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="The circum-basmati group of cultivated Asian rice (Oryza sativa) contains many iconic varieties and is widespread in the Indian subcontinent. Despite its economic and cultural importance, a high-quality reference genome is currently lacking, and the groups evolutionary history is not fully resolved. To address these gaps, we use long-read nanopore sequencing and assemble the genomes of two circum-basmati rice varieties. We generate two high-quality, chromosome-level reference genomes that represent the 12 chromosomes of Oryza. The assemblies show a contig N50 of 6.32Mb and 10.53Mb for Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid, respectively. Using our highly contiguous assemblies, we characterize structural variations segregating across circum-basmati genomes. We discover repeat expansions not observed in japonicathe rice group most closely related to circum-basmatias well as the presence and absence variants of over 20Mb, one of which is a circum-basmati-specific deletion of a gene regulating awn length. We further detect strong evidence of admixture between the circum-basmati and circum-aus groups. This gene flow has its greatest effect on chromosome 10, causing both structural variation and single-nucleotide polymorphism to deviate from genome-wide history. Lastly, population genomic analysis of 78 circum-basmati varieties shows three major geographically structured genetic groups: Bhutan/Nepal, India/Bangladesh/Myanmar, and Iran/Pakistan. The availability of high-quality reference genomes allows functional and evolutionary genomic analyses providing genome-wide evidence for gene flow between circum-aus and circum-basmati, describes the nature of circum-basmati structural variation, and reveals the presence/absence variation in this important and iconic rice variety group."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/13059.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>The circum-basmati group of cultivated Asian rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i>) contains many iconic varieties and is widespread in the Indian subcontinent. Despite its economic and cultural importance, a high-quality reference genome is currently lacking, and the groups evolutionary history is not fully resolved. To address these gaps, we use long-read nanopore sequencing and assemble the genomes of two circum-basmati rice varieties.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>We generate two high-quality, chromosome-level reference genomes that represent the 12 chromosomes of <i>Oryza</i>. The assemblies show a contig N50 of 6.32Mb and 10.53Mb for Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid, respectively. Using our highly contiguous assemblies, we characterize structural variations segregating across circum-basmati genomes. We discover repeat expansions not observed in japonicathe rice group most closely related to circum-basmatias well as the presence and absence variants of over 20Mb, one of which is a circum-basmati-specific deletion of a gene regulating awn length. We further detect strong evidence of admixture between the circum-basmati and circum-aus groups. This gene flow has its greatest effect on chromosome 10, causing both structural variation and single-nucleotide polymorphism to deviate from genome-wide history. Lastly, population genomic analysis of 78 circum-basmati varieties shows three major geographically structured genetic groups: Bhutan/Nepal, India/Bangladesh/Myanmar, and Iran/Pakistan.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusion</h3><p>The availability of high-quality reference genomes allows functional and evolutionary genomic analyses providing genome-wide evidence for gene flow between circum-aus and circum-basmati, describes the nature of circum-basmati structural variation, and reveals the presence/absence variation in this important and iconic rice variety group.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Oryza sativa</i> or Asian rice is an agriculturally important crop that feeds one-half of the worlds population [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Gnanamanickam SS. Rice and its importance to human life. In: Biological control of rice diseases. Dordrecht: Springer Netherlands; 2009. p. 111. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/978-90-481-2465-7_1&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14746e581">1</a>] and supplies 20% of peoples caloric intake (<a href="http://www.fao.org">www.fao.org</a>). Historically, <i>O. sativa</i> has been classified into two major variety groups, japonica and indica, based on morphometric differences and molecular markers [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Matsuo T, Futsuhara Y, Kikuchi F, Yamaguchi H. Science of the rice plant. Tokyo: Food and Agriculture Policy Research Center; 1997." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14746e591">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Gross BL, Zhao Z. Archaeological and genetic insights into the origins of domesticated rice. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2014;111:61907." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d14746e594">3</a>]. These variety groups can be considered as subspecies, particularly given the presence of reproductive barriers between them [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Nadir S, Khan S, Zhu Q, Henry D, Wei L, Lee DS, et al. An overview on reproductive isolation in Oryza sativa complex. AoB Plants. 2018;10:ply060." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14746e598">4</a>]. Archaeobotanical remains suggest japonica rice was domesticated ~9000years ago in the Yangtze Basin of China, while indica rice originated ~4000years ago when domestication alleles were introduced from japonica into either <i>O. nivara</i> or a proto-indica in the Indian subcontinent [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Fuller DQ, Sato Y-I, Castillo C, Qin L, Weisskopf AR, Kingwell-Banham EJ, et al. Consilience of genetics and archaeobotany in the entangled history of rice. Archaeol Anthropol Sci. 2010;2:11531." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14746e604">5</a>]. More recently, two additional variety groups have been recognized that are genetically distinct from japonica and indica: the aus/circum-aus and aromatic/circum-basmati rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Garris AJ, Tai TH, Coburn J, Kresovich S, McCouch S. Genetic structure and diversity in Oryza sativa L. Genetics. 2005;169:16318." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14746e607">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557:439." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14746e607_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Glaszmann JC. Isozymes and classification of Asian rice varieties. Theoret Appl Genetics. 1987;74:2130." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14746e610">8</a>].</p><p>The rich genetic diversity of Asian rice is likely a result from a complex domestication process involving multiple wild progenitor populations and the exchange of important domestication alleles between <i>O. sativa</i> variety groups through gene flow [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Fuller DQ, Sato Y-I, Castillo C, Qin L, Weisskopf AR, Kingwell-Banham EJ, et al. Consilience of genetics and archaeobotany in the entangled history of rice. Archaeol Anthropol Sci. 2010;2:11531." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14746e619">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557:439." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14746e622">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="He Z, Zhai W, Wen H, Tang T, Wang Y, Lu X, et al. Two evolutionary histories in the genome of rice: the roles of domestication genes. PLoS Genet. 2011;7:e1002100." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14746e625">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fuller DQ. Pathways to Asian civilizations: tracing the origins and spread of rice and rice cultures. Rice. 2012;4:7892." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14746e625_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Meyer RS, Purugganan MD. Evolution of crop species: genetics of domestication and diversification. Nat Rev Genet. 2013;14:84052." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14746e625_2">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang X, Han B. Rice domestication occurred through single origin and multiple introgressions. Nat Plants. 2015;2:15207." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14746e625_3">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Castillo CC, Tanaka K, Sato Y-I, Ishikawa R, Bellina B, Higham C, et al. Archaeogenetic study of prehistoric rice remains from Thailand and India: evidence of early japonica in South and Southeast Asia. Archaeol Anthropol Sci. 2016;8:52343." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14746e625_4">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Choi JY, Platts AE, Fuller DQ, Hsing Y-I, Wing RA, Purugganan MD. The rice paradox: multiple origins but single domestication in Asian rice. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:96979." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14746e625_5">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Choi JY, Purugganan MD. Multiple origin but single domestication led to Oryza sativa. G3: genes, genomes. Genetics. 2018;8:797803." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14746e625_6">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang X, Kurata N, Wei X, Wang Z-X, Wang A, Zhao Q, et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature. 2012;490:497501." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14746e625_7">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Civ P, Craig H, Cox CJ, Brown TA. Three geographically separate domestications of Asian rice. Nat Plants. 2015;1:15164." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14746e628">17</a>]. Moreover, many agricultural traits within rice are variety group-specific [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang ZY, Zheng FQ, Shen GZ, Gao JP, Snustad DP, Li MG, et al. The amylose content in rice endosperm is related to the post-transcriptional regulation of the waxy gene. Plant J. 1995;7:61322." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d14746e632">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sweeney MT, Thomson MJ, Pfeil BE, McCouch S. Caught red-handed: Rc encodes a basic helix-loop-helix protein conditioning red pericarp in rice. Plant Cell. 2006;18:28394." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14746e632_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Konishi S, Izawa T, Lin SY, Ebana K, Fukuta Y, Sasaki T, et al. An SNP caused loss of seed shattering during rice domestication. Science. 2006;312:13926." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14746e632_2">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kovach MJ, Calingacion MN, Fitzgerald MA, McCouch SR. The origin and evolution of fragrance in rice (Oryza sativa L.). Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2009;106:144449." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14746e632_3">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xu K, Xu X, Fukao T, Canlas P, Maghirang-Rodriguez R, Heuer S, et al. Sub1A is an ethylene-response-factor-like gene that confers submergence tolerance to rice. Nature. 2006;442:7058." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14746e632_4">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Bin Rahman ANMR, Zhang J. Preferential geographic distribution pattern of abiotic stress tolerant rice. Rice. 2018;11:10." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14746e635">23</a>], suggesting local adaptation to environments or cultural preferences have partially driven the diversification of rice varieties.</p><p>Arguably, the circum-basmati rice group has been the least studied among the four major variety groups, and it was only recently defined in more detail based on insights from genomic data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557:439." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14746e641">7</a>]. Among its members, the group boasts the iconic basmati rice (sensu stricto) from southern Asia and the sadri rice from Iran [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Garris AJ, Tai TH, Coburn J, Kresovich S, McCouch S. Genetic structure and diversity in Oryza sativa L. Genetics. 2005;169:16318." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14746e644">6</a>]. Many, but not all, circum-basmati varieties are characterized by distinct and highly desirable fragrance and texture [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Singh R, Singh U, Khush G. Aromatic rices. New Delhi: Oxford &amp; IBH Publishing Co Pvt Ltd; 2000." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14746e647">24</a>]. Nearly all fragrant circum-basmati varieties possess a loss-of-function mutation in the <i>BADH2</i> gene that has its origins in ancestral japonica haplotypes, suggesting that an introgression between circum-basmati and japonica may have led to fragrant basmati rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kovach MJ, Calingacion MN, Fitzgerald MA, McCouch SR. The origin and evolution of fragrance in rice (Oryza sativa L.). Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2009;106:144449." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14746e653">21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bradbury LMT, Gillies SA, Brushett DJ, Waters DLE, Henry RJ. Inactivation of an aminoaldehyde dehydrogenase is responsible for fragrance in rice. Plant Mol Biol. 2008;68:43949." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14746e657">25</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chen S, Yang Y, Shi W, Ji Q, He F, Zhang Z, et al. Badh2, encoding betaine aldehyde dehydrogenase, inhibits the biosynthesis of 2-acetyl-1-pyrroline, a major component in rice fragrance. Plant Cell. 2008;20:185061." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d14746e660">26</a>]. Genome-wide polymorphism analysis of a smaller array of circum-basmati rice cultivars shows close association with japonica varieties [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557:439." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14746e663">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Huang X, Kurata N, Wei X, Wang Z-X, Wang A, Zhao Q, et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature. 2012;490:497501." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14746e666">16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zhao K, Tung C-W, Eizenga GC, Wright MH, Ali ML, Price AH, et al. Genome-wide association mapping reveals a rich genetic architecture of complex traits in Oryza sativa. Nat Commun. 2011;2:467." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d14746e669">27</a>], providing evidence that at least part of the genomic make-up of circum-basmati rice may indeed be traced back to japonica.</p><p>Whole-genome sequences are an important resource for evolutionary geneticists studying plant domestication, as well as breeders aiming to improve crop varieties. Single-molecule sequencing regularly produces sequencing reads in the range of kilobases (kb) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Heather JM, Chain B. The sequence of sequencers: the history of sequencing DNA. Genomics. 2016;107:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d14746e675">28</a>]. This is particularly helpful for assembling plant genomes, which are often highly repetitive and heterozygous, and commonly underwent at least one round of polyploidization in the past [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Michael TP, VanBuren R. Progress, challenges and the future of crop genomes. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 2015;24:7181." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d14746e678">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiao W-B, Schneeberger K. The impact of third generation genomic technologies on plant genome assembly. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 2017;36:6470." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d14746e678_1">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Li C, Lin F, An D, Wang W, Huang R. Genome sequencing and assembly by long reads in plants. Genes. 2017;9. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.3390/genes9010006&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d14746e681">31</a>]. The <i>Oryza sativa</i> genome, with a relatively modest size of ~400Mb, was the first crop genome sequence assembled [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Michael TP, VanBuren R. Progress, challenges and the future of crop genomes. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 2015;24:7181." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d14746e687">29</a>], and there has been much progress in generating de novo genome assemblies for other members of the genus <i>Oryza</i>. Currently, there are assemblies for nine wild species (<i>Leersia perrieri</i> [outgroup], <i>O. barthii</i>, <i>O. brachyantha</i>, <i>O. glumaepatula</i>, <i>O. longistaminata</i>, <i>O. meridionalis</i>, <i>O. nivara</i>, <i>O. punctata</i>, and <i>O. rufipogon</i>) and two domesticated species (<i>O. glaberrima</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yu J, Hu S, Wang J, Wong GK-S, Li S, Liu B, et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science. 2002;296:7992." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14746e729">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature. 2005;436:793800." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14746e729_1">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen J, Huang Q, Gao D, Wang J, Lang Y, Liu T, et al. Whole-genome sequencing of Oryza brachyantha reveals mechanisms underlying Oryza genome evolution. Nat Commun. 2013;4:1595." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d14746e729_2">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang M, Yu Y, Haberer G, Marri PR, Fan C, Goicoechea JL, et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat Genet. 2014;46:9828." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14746e729_3">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang Y, Zhang S, Liu H, Fu B, Li L, Xie M, et al. Genome and comparative transcriptomics of African wild rice Oryza longistaminata provide insights into molecular mechanism of rhizomatousness and self-incompatibility. Mol Plant. 2015;8:16836." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d14746e729_4">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e732">37</a>].</p><p>Within domesticated Asian rice (<i>O. sativa</i>), genome assemblies are available for cultivars in most variety groups [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yu J, Hu S, Wang J, Wong GK-S, Li S, Liu B, et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science. 2002;296:7992." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14746e742">32</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature. 2005;436:793800." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14746e745">33</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schatz MC, Maron LG, Stein JC, Wences A, Gurtowski J, Biggers E, et al. Whole genome de novo assemblies of three divergent strains of rice, Oryza sativa , document novel gene space of aus and indica. Genome Biol. 2014;15:506." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d14746e748">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sakai H, Kanamori H, Arai-Kichise Y, Shibata-Hatta M, Ebana K, Oono Y, et al. Construction of pseudomolecule sequences of the aus rice cultivar Kasalath for comparative genomics of Asian cultivated rice. DNA Res. 2014;21:397405." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d14746e748_1">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang J, Chen L-L, Xing F, Kudrna DA, Yao W, Copetti D, et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2016;113:E516371." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14746e748_2">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Du H, Yu Y, Ma Y, Gao Q, Cao Y, Chen Z, et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:15324." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14746e748_3">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhao Q, Feng Q, Lu H, Li Y, Wang A, Tian Q, et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat Genet. 2018;50:27884." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14746e751">42</a>]. However, several of these reference assemblies are based on short-read sequencing data and show higher levels of incompleteness compared to assemblies generated from long-read sequences [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang J, Chen L-L, Xing F, Kudrna DA, Yao W, Copetti D, et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2016;113:E516371." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14746e755">40</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Du H, Yu Y, Ma Y, Gao Q, Cao Y, Chen Z, et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:15324." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14746e758">41</a>]. Nevertheless, these de novo genome assemblies have been critical in revealing genomic variation (e.g., variations in genome structure and repetitive DNA, and de novo species- or population-specific genes) that were otherwise missed from analyzing a single reference genome. Recently, a genome assembly based on short-read sequencing data was generated for basmati rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhao Q, Feng Q, Lu H, Li Y, Wang A, Tian Q, et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat Genet. 2018;50:27884." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14746e761">42</a>]. Not only were there missing sequences in this assembly, it was also generated from DNA of an elite basmati breeding line. Such modern cultivars are not the best foundations for domestication-related analyses due to higher levels of introgression from other rice populations during modern breeding.</p><p>Here, we report the de novo sequencing and assembly of the landraces (traditional varieties) Basmati 334 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kovach MJ, Calingacion MN, Fitzgerald MA, McCouch SR. The origin and evolution of fragrance in rice (Oryza sativa L.). Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2009;106:144449." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14746e767">21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Jain S, Jain RK, McCouch SR. Genetic analysis of Indian aromatic and quality rice (Oryza sativa L.) germplasm using panels of fluorescently-labeled microsatellite markers. Theor Appl Genet. 2004;109:96577." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d14746e770">43</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Vikram P, Swamy BPM, Dixit S, Ahmed H, Cruz MTS, Singh AK, et al. Bulk segregant analysis: An effective approach for mapping consistent-effect drought grain yield QTLs in rice.. Field Crop Res. 2012;134:18592." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d14746e773">44</a>] and Dom Sufid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kovach MJ, Calingacion MN, Fitzgerald MA, McCouch SR. The origin and evolution of fragrance in rice (Oryza sativa L.). Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2009;106:144449." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14746e776">21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Singh R, Singh U, Khush G. Aromatic rices. New Delhi: Oxford &amp; IBH Publishing Co Pvt Ltd; 2000." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14746e779">24</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="McNally KL, Childs KL, Bohnert R, Davidson RM, Zhao K, Ulat VJ, et al. Genomewide SNP variation reveals relationships among landraces and modern varieties of rice. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2009;106:122738." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d14746e783">45</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="McNally KL, Bruskiewich R, Mackill D, Buell CR, Leach JE, Leung H. Sequencing multiple and diverse rice varieties. Connecting whole-genome variation with phenotypes. Plant Physiol. 2006;141:2631." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d14746e786">46</a>] using the long-read nanopore sequencing platform of Oxford Nanopore Technologies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Jain M, Olsen HE, Paten B, Akeson M. The Oxford Nanopore MinION: delivery of nanopore sequencing to the genomics community. Genome Biol. 2016;17:239." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d14746e789">47</a>]. Basmati 334 is from Pakistan, evolved in a rainfed lowland environment and is known to be drought tolerant at the seedling and reproductive stages [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Vikram P, Swamy BPM, Dixit S, Ahmed H, Cruz MTS, Singh AK, et al. Bulk segregant analysis: An effective approach for mapping consistent-effect drought grain yield QTLs in rice.. Field Crop Res. 2012;134:18592." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d14746e792">44</a>]. It also possesses several broad-spectrum bacterial blight resistance alleles [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Chen S, Huang Z, Zeng L, Yang J, Liu Q, Zhu X. High-resolution mapping and gene prediction of Xanthomonas Oryzae pv. Oryzae resistance gene Xa7. Mol Breed. 2008;22:43341." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d14746e795">48</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ullah I, Jamil S, Iqbal MZ, Shaheen HL, Hasni SM, Jabeen S, et al. Detection of bacterial blight resistance genes in basmati rice landraces. Genet Mol Res. 2012;11:19606." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d14746e798">49</a>], making Basmati 334 desirable for breeding resilience into modern basmati cultivars [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ullah I, Jamil S, Iqbal MZ, Shaheen HL, Hasni SM, Jabeen S, et al. Detection of bacterial blight resistance genes in basmati rice landraces. Genet Mol Res. 2012;11:19606." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d14746e802">49</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Sandhu N, Kumar A, Sandhu N, Kumar A. Bridging the rice yield gaps under drought: QTLs, genes, and their use in breeding programs. Agronomy. 2017;7:27." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d14746e805">50</a>]. Dom Sufid is an Iranian sadri cultivar that, like other sadri and basmati (sensu stricto) varieties, is among the most expensive varieties currently available in the market [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Singh R, Singh U, Khush G. Aromatic rices. New Delhi: Oxford &amp; IBH Publishing Co Pvt Ltd; 2000." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14746e808">24</a>]. It has desirable characteristics such as aromaticity and grain elongation during cooking, although it is susceptible to disease and abiotic stress [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Singh R, Singh U, Khush G. Aromatic rices. New Delhi: Oxford &amp; IBH Publishing Co Pvt Ltd; 2000." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14746e811">24</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Henry A, Gowda VRP, Torres RO, McNally KL, Serraj R. Variation in root system architecture and drought response in rice (Oryza sativa): Phenotyping of the OryzaSNP panel in rainfed lowland fields. Field Crop Res. 2011;120:20514." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d14746e814">51</a>]. Because of their special characteristics, both Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid are used in elite rice breeding programs to create high yielding and resilient aromatic rice varieties [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Singh R, Singh U, Khush G. Aromatic rices. New Delhi: Oxford &amp; IBH Publishing Co Pvt Ltd; 2000." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14746e817">24</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vikram P, Swamy BPM, Dixit S, Ahmed H, Cruz MTS, Singh AK, et al. Bulk segregant analysis: An effective approach for mapping consistent-effect drought grain yield QTLs in rice.. Field Crop Res. 2012;134:18592." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d14746e821">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="McNally KL, Childs KL, Bohnert R, Davidson RM, Zhao K, Ulat VJ, et al. Genomewide SNP variation reveals relationships among landraces and modern varieties of rice. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2009;106:122738." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d14746e821_1">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="McNally KL, Bruskiewich R, Mackill D, Buell CR, Leach JE, Leung H. Sequencing multiple and diverse rice varieties. Connecting whole-genome variation with phenotypes. Plant Physiol. 2006;141:2631." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d14746e824">46</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Sandhu N, Kumar A, Sandhu N, Kumar A. Bridging the rice yield gaps under drought: QTLs, genes, and their use in breeding programs. Agronomy. 2017;7:27." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d14746e827">50</a>].</p><p>Based on long reads from nanopore sequencing, our genome assemblies have high quality, contiguity, and genic completeness, making them comparable in quality to assemblies associated with key rice reference genomes. We used our circum-basmati genome assemblies to characterize genomic variation existing within this important rice variety group, and analyze domestication-related and other evolutionary processes that shaped this variation. Our circum-basmati rice genome assemblies will be valuable complements to the available assemblies for other rice cultivars, unlocking important genomic variation for rice crop improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Nanopore sequencing of basmati and sadri rice</h3><p>Using Oxford Nanopore Technologies long-read sequencing platform, we sequenced the genomes of the circum-basmati landraces Basmati 334 (basmati sensu stricto) and Dom Sufid (sadri). We called 1,372,950 reads constituting a total of 29.2Gb for Basmati 334 and 1,183,159 reads constituting a total of 24.2Gb for Dom Sufid (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Tab1">1</a>). For both samples, the median read length was &gt;17kb, the read length N50 was &gt;33kb, and the median quality score per read was ~11.
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of nanopore sequencing read data</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">De novo assembly of the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid rice genomes</h3><p>Incorporating only those reads that had a mean quality score of &gt;8 and read lengths of &gt;8kb, we used a total of 1,076,192 reads and 902,040 reads for the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid genome assemblies, which resulted in a genome coverage of ~62 and ~51, respectively (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Tab2">2</a>). We polished the genome assemblies with both nanopore and short Illumina sequencing reads. The final, polished genome assemblies spanned 386.5Mb across 188 contigs for Basmati 334 and 383.6Mb across 116 contigs for Dom Sufid. The genome assemblies had high contiguity, with a contig N50 of 6.32Mb and 10.53Mb for Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid, respectively. Our genome assemblies recovered more than 97% of the 1440 BUSCO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Cantarel BL, Korf I, Robb SMC, Parra G, Ross E, Moore B, et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 2008;18:18896." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1038">52</a>] embryophyte gene groups, which is comparable to the BUSCO statistics for the japonica Nipponbare [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature. 2005;436:793800." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1041">33</a>] (98.4%) and indica R498 reference genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Du H, Yu Y, Ma Y, Gao Q, Cao Y, Chen Z, et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:15324." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1044">41</a>] (98.0%). This is an improvement from the currently available genome assembly of basmati variety GP295-1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhao Q, Feng Q, Lu H, Li Y, Wang A, Tian Q, et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat Genet. 2018;50:27884." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1047">42</a>], which was generated from Illumina short-read sequencing data and has a contig N50 of 44.4kb with 50,786 assembled contigs.
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Summary of the circum-basmati rice genome assemblies</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We examined coding sequences of our circum-basmati genomes by conducting gene annotation using published rice gene models and the <i>MAKER</i> gene annotation pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Cantarel BL, Korf I, Robb SMC, Parra G, Ross E, Moore B, et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 2008;18:18896." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1166">52</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Holt C, Yandell M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:491." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1169">53</a>]. A total of 41,270 genes were annotated for the Basmati 334 genome, and 38,329 for the Dom Sufid genome. BUSCO gene completion analysis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Cantarel BL, Korf I, Robb SMC, Parra G, Ross E, Moore B, et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 2008;18:18896." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1172">52</a>] indicated that 95.4% and 93.6% of the 3278 single-copy genes from the liliopsida gene dataset were found in the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid gene annotations, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Whole-genome comparison to other rice variety group genomes</h3><p>We aligned our draft genome assemblies to the japonica Nipponbare reference genome sequence [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature. 2005;436:793800." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1183">33</a>], which represents one of the highest quality reference genome sequences (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig1">1</a>a). Between the Nipponbare, Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid genomes, high levels of macro-synteny were evident across the japonica chromosomes. Specifically, we observed little large-scale structural variation between Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid contigs and the japonica genome. A noticeable exception was an apparent inversion in the circum-basmati genome assemblies at chromosome 6 between positions 12.5 and 18.7Mb (Nipponbare coordinates), corresponding to the pericentromeric region [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Choi JY, Purugganan MD. Evolutionary epigenomics of retrotransposon-mediated methylation spreading in rice. Mol Biol Evol. 2018;35:36582." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1189">54</a>]. Interestingly, the same region showed an inversion between the Nipponbare and indica R498 reference genomes [41], whereas in the circum-aus N22 cultivar no inversions are observed (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S1). While the entire region was inverted in R498, the inversion positions were disjoint in Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid, apparently occurring in multiple regions of the pericentromere. We independently verified the inversions by aligning raw nanopore sequencing reads to the Nipponbare reference genome using the long-read-aware aligner <i>ngmlr</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Sedlazeck FJ, Rescheneder P, Smolka M, Fang H, Nattestad M, von Haeseler A, et al. Accurate detection of complex structural variations using single-molecule sequencing. Nat Methods. 2018;15:4618." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1199">55</a>], and the structural variation detection program <i>sniffles</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Sedlazeck FJ, Rescheneder P, Smolka M, Fang H, Nattestad M, von Haeseler A, et al. Accurate detection of complex structural variations using single-molecule sequencing. Nat Methods. 2018;15:4618." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1205">55</a>]. <i>Sniffles</i> detected several inversions, including a large inversion between positions 13.1 and 17.7Mb and between 18.18 and 18.23Mb, with several smaller inversions located within the largest inversion (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S1).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="660" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Dot plot comparing the assembly contigs of Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid to <b>a</b> all chromosomes of the Nipponbare genome assembly and <b>b</b> only chromosome 6 of Nipponbare. Only alignment blocks with greater than 80% sequence identity are shown</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Because of high macro-synteny with japonica (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig1">1</a>a), we ordered and oriented the contigs of the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid assemblies using a reference genome-based scaffolding approach [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Alonge M, Soyk S, Ramakrishnan S, Wang X, Goodwin S, Sedlazeck FJ, et al. RaGOO: fast and accurate reference-guided scaffolding of draft genomes. Genome Biol. 2019;20:224." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1234">56</a>]. For both Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid, over 99.9% of the assembled genomic contigs were anchored to the Nipponbare reference genome (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Tab2">2</a>). The scaffolded circum-basmati chromosomes were similar in size to those in reference genomes for cultivars in other rice variety groups (Nipponbare [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature. 2005;436:793800." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1240">33</a>], the circum-aus variety N22 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1243">37</a>], and the indica varieties IR8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1247">37</a>] and R498 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Du H, Yu Y, Ma Y, Gao Q, Cao Y, Chen Z, et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:15324." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1250">41</a>]) that were sequenced, assembled, and scaffolded to near completion (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Tab3">3</a>).
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Comparison of assembled chromosome sizes for cultivars across variety groups</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Next, we assessed the assembly quality of the circum-basmati genomes by contrasting them against available de novo-assembled genomes within the Asian rice complex (see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Sec16">Materials and methods</a> section for a complete list of genomes). We generated a multi-genome alignment to the Nipponbare genome, which we chose as the reference since its assembly and gene annotation is a product of years of community-based efforts [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature. 2005;436:793800." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1488">33</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Kawahara Y, de la Bastide M, Hamilton JP, Kanamori H, McCombie WR, Ouyang S, et al. Improvement of the Oryza sativa Nipponbare reference genome using next generation sequence and optical map data. Rice. 2013;6:4." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1491">57</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Sakai H, Lee SS, Tanaka T, Numa H, Kim J, Kawahara Y, et al. Rice annotation project database (RAP-DB): an integrative and interactive database for rice genomics. Plant Cell Physiol. 2013;54:e6." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1494">58</a>]. To infer the quality of the gene regions in each of the genome assemblies, we used the multi-genome alignment to extract the coding DNA sequence of each Nipponbare gene and its orthologous regions from each non-japonica genome. The orthologous genes were counted for missing DNA sequences (N sequences) and gaps to estimate the percent of Nipponbare genes covered. For all genomes, the majority of Nipponbare genes had a near-zero proportion of sites that were missing in the orthologous non-Nipponbare genes (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S2). The missing proportions of Nipponbare-orthologous genes within the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid genomes were comparable to those for genomes that had higher assembly contiguity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1501">37</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang J, Chen L-L, Xing F, Kudrna DA, Yao W, Copetti D, et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2016;113:E516371." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1504">40</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Du H, Yu Y, Ma Y, Gao Q, Cao Y, Chen Z, et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:15324." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1507">41</a>].</p><p>Focusing on the previously sequenced basmati GP295-1 genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhao Q, Feng Q, Lu H, Li Y, Wang A, Tian Q, et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat Genet. 2018;50:27884." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1513">42</a>], our newly assembled circum-basmati genomes had noticeably lower proportions of missing genes (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S2). Furthermore, over 96% of base pairs across the Nipponbare genome were alignable against the Basmati 334 (total of 359,557,873bp [96.33%] of Nipponbare genome) or Dom Sufid (total of 359,819,239bp [96.40%] of Nipponbare genome) assemblies, while only 194,464,958bp (52.1%) of the Nipponbare genome were alignable against the GP295-1 assembly.</p><p>We then counted the single-nucleotide and insertion/deletion (indel, up to ~60bp) differences between the circum-basmati and Nipponbare assemblies to assess the overall quality of our newly assembled genomes. To avoid analyzing differences across unconstrained repeat regions, we specifically examined regions where there were 20 exact base-pair matches flanking a site that had a single-nucleotide or indel difference between the circum-basmati and Nipponbare genomes. In the GP295-1 genome, there were 334,500 (0.17%) single-nucleotide differences and 44,609 (0.023%) indels compared to the Nipponbare genome. Our newly assembled genomes had similar proportions of single-nucleotide differences with the Nipponbare genome, where the Basmati 334 genome had 780,735 (0.22%) differences and the Dom Sufid genome had 731,426 (0.20%). For indels, the Basmati 334 genome had comparable proportions of differences with 104,282 (0.029%) variants, but the Dom Sufid genome had higher proportions with 222,813 (0.062%) variants. In sum, our draft circum-basmati genomes had high contiguity and completeness as evidenced by assembly to the chromosome level and comparison to the Nipponbare genome. In addition, our genome assemblies were comparable to the Illumina sequence-generated GP295-1 genome for the proportion of genomic differences with the Nipponbare genome, suggesting they had high quality and accuracy as well.</p><p>Our circum-basmati genome assemblies should also be of sufficiently high quality for detailed gene-level analysis. For instance, a hallmark of many circum-basmati rice is aromaticity, and a previous study had determined that Dom Sufid, but not Basmati 334, is a fragrant variety [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kovach MJ, Calingacion MN, Fitzgerald MA, McCouch SR. The origin and evolution of fragrance in rice (Oryza sativa L.). Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2009;106:144449." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1526">21</a>]. We examined the two genomes to verify the presence or absence of the mutations associated with fragrance. There are multiple different loss-of-function mutations in the <i>BADH2</i> gene that cause rice varieties to be fragrant [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kovach MJ, Calingacion MN, Fitzgerald MA, McCouch SR. The origin and evolution of fragrance in rice (Oryza sativa L.). Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2009;106:144449." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1532">21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bradbury LMT, Gillies SA, Brushett DJ, Waters DLE, Henry RJ. Inactivation of an aminoaldehyde dehydrogenase is responsible for fragrance in rice. Plant Mol Biol. 2008;68:43949." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1535">25</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chen S, Yang Y, Shi W, Ji Q, He F, Zhang Z, et al. Badh2, encoding betaine aldehyde dehydrogenase, inhibits the biosynthesis of 2-acetyl-1-pyrroline, a major component in rice fragrance. Plant Cell. 2008;20:185061." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1538">26</a>], but the majority of fragrant rice carry a deletion of 8 nucleotides at position chr8:20,382,861-20,382,868 of the Nipponbare genome assembly (version Os-Nipponbare-Reference-IRGSP-1.0). Using the genome alignment, we extracted the <i>BADH2</i> sequence region to compare the gene sequence of the non-fragrant Nipponbare to that of Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid. Consistent with previous observations [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kovach MJ, Calingacion MN, Fitzgerald MA, McCouch SR. The origin and evolution of fragrance in rice (Oryza sativa L.). Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2009;106:144449." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1545">21</a>], we found that the genome of the non-fragrant Basmati 334 did not carry the deletion and contained the wild-type <i>BADH2</i> haplotype observed in Nipponbare. The genome of the fragrant Dom Sufid, on the other hand, carried the 8-bp deletion, as well as the 3 single-nucleotide polymorphisms flanking the deletion. This illustrates that the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid genomes are accurate enough for gene-level analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Circum-basmati gene analysis</h3><p>Our annotation identified ~40,000 coding sequences in the circum-basmati assemblies. We examined population frequencies of the annotated gene models across a circum-basmati population dataset to filter out mis-annotated gene models or genes at very low frequency in a population. We obtained Illumina sequencing reads from varieties included in the 3K Rice Genome Project [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557:439." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1559">7</a>] and sequenced additional varieties to analyze a total of 78 circum-basmati cultivars (see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S2 for a list of varieties). The Illumina sequencing reads were aligned to the circum-basmati genomes, and if the average coverage of a genic region was &lt;0.05 for an individual, this gene was called as a deletion in that variety. Since we used a low threshold for calling a deletion, the genome-wide sequencing coverage of a variety did not influence the number of gene deletions detected (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S3). Results showed that gene deletions were indeed rare across the circum-basmati population (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig2">2</a>a), consistent with their probable deleterious nature. We found that 31,565 genes (76.5%) in Basmati 334 and 29,832 genes (77.8%) in the Dom Sufid genomes did not have a deletion across the population (see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S3 for a list of genes).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="949" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Circum-basmati gene sequence evolution. <b>a</b> The deletion frequency of genes annotated from the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid genomes. Frequency was estimated from sequencing data on a population of 78 circum-basmati varieties. <b>b</b> Groups of orthologous and paralogous genes (i.e., orthogroups) identified in the reference genomes of circum-aus N22, japonica Nipponbare (NPB), and indica R498, as well as the circum-basmati genome assemblies Basmati 334 (B334) and Dom Sufid (DS) of this study. <b>c</b> Visualization of the genomic region orthologous to the Nipponbare gene Os03g0418600 (<i>Awn3-1</i>) in the N22, Basmati 334, and Dom Sufid genomes. Regions orthologous to <i>Awn3-1</i> are indicated with a dotted box</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>There were 517 gene models from Basmati 334 and 431 gene models from Dom Sufid that had a deletion frequency of 0.3 (see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S4 for a list of genes). These gene models with high deletion frequencies were not considered further in this analysis. The rest were compared against the circum-aus N22, indica R498, and japonica Nipponbare gene models to determine their orthogroup status (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig2">2</a>b; see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S5 for a list of genes and their orthogroup status), which are sets of genes that are orthologs and recent paralogs of each other [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Emms DM, Kelly S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 2015;16:157." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1610">59</a>].</p><p>The most frequent orthogroup class observed was for groups in which every rice variety group has at least one gene member. There were 13,894 orthogroups within this class, consisting of 17,361 genes from N22, 18,302 genes from Basmati 334, 17,936 genes from Dom Sufid, 17,553 genes from R498, and 18,351 genes from Nipponbare. This orthogroup class likely represents the set of core genes of <i>O. sativa</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhao Q, Feng Q, Lu H, Li Y, Wang A, Tian Q, et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat Genet. 2018;50:27884." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1619">42</a>]. The second-highest orthogroup class observed was for groups with genes that were uniquely found in both circum-basmati genomes (3802 orthogroups). These genes represent those restricted to the circum-basmati group.</p><p>In comparison to genes in other rice variety groups, the circum-basmati genes shared the highest number of orthogroups with circum-aus (2648 orthogroups), followed by japonica (1378 orthogroups), while sharing the lowest number of orthogroups with indica (663 orthogroups). In fact, genes from indica variety R498 had the lowest number assigned to an orthogroup (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig2">2</a>b inset table), suggesting this genome had more unique genes, i.e., without orthologs/paralogs to genes in other rice variety groups.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome-wide presence/absence variation within the circum-basmati genomes</h3><p>Our assembled circum-basmati genomes were&gt;10Mb longer than the Nipponbare genome, but individual chromosomes showed different relative lengths (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Tab3">3</a>) suggesting a considerable number of presence/absence variants (PAVs) between the genomes. We examined the PAVs between the circum-basmati and Nipponbare genomes using two different computational packages: (i) <i>sniffles</i>, which uses raw nanopore reads aligned to a reference genome to call PAVs, and (ii) <i>assemblytics</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Nattestad M, Schatz MC. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics. 2016;32:30213." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1646">60</a>], which aligns the genome assemblies to each other and calls PAVs. The results showed that, while the total number of PAVs called by <i>sniffles</i> and <i>assemblytics</i> were similar, only ~36% of PAVs had overlapping positions (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Tab4">4</a>). In addition, the combined total size of PAVs was larger for predictions made by <i>sniffles</i> compared to those by <i>assemblytics</i>. For subsequent analysis, we focused on PAVs that were called by both methods.
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Comparison of presence/absence variation called by two different computational packages</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The distribution of PAV sizes indicated that large PAVs were rare across the circum-basmati genomes, while PAVs &lt;500bps in size were the most common (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig3">3</a>a). Within smaller-sized PAVs those in the 200500-bp size range showed a peak in abundance. A closer examination revealed that sequence positions of more than 75% of these 200500-bp-sized PAVs overlapped with transposable element coordinates in the circum-basmati genomes (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S6). A previous study based on short-read Illumina sequencing data reported a similar enrichment of short repetitive elements such as the long terminal repeats (LTRs) of retrotransposons, <i>Tc1/mariner</i> elements, and <i>mPing</i> elements among PAVs in this size range [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Fuentes RR, Chebotarov D, Duitama J, Smith S, la Hoz JFD, Mohiyuddin M, et al. Structural variants in 3000 rice genomes. Genome Res. 2019;29:87080." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1782">61</a>].
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a
