class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="847" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Presence/absence variation across the circum-basmati rice genome assemblies. <b>a</b> Distribution of the presence/absence variant sizes compared to the japonica Nipponbare reference genome. <b>b</b> Number of presence/absence variants that are shared between or unique for the circum-basmati genomes. <b>c</b> Chromosome-wide distribution of presence/absence variation for each circum-basmati rice genome, relative to the Nipponbare genome coordinates</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>PAVs shorter than 200bps also overlapped with repetitive sequence positions in the circum-basmati genomes, but the relative abundance of each repeat type differed among insertion and deletion variants. Insertions in the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid genomes had a higher relative abundance of simple sequence repeats (i.e., microsatellites) compared to deletions (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S6). These inserted simple sequence repeats were highly enriched for (AT)<sub>n</sub> dinucleotide repeats, which in Basmati 334 accounted for 66,624bps out of a total of 72,436bps (92.0%) of simple sequence repeats, and for Dom Sufid 56,032bps out of a total of 63,127bps (88.8%).</p><p>Between the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid genomes, ~45% of PAVs had overlapping genome coordinates (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig3">3</a>b) suggesting that variety-specific insertion and deletion polymorphisms were common. We plotted PAVs for each of our circum-basmati genomes to visualize their distribution (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig3">3</a>c). Chromosome-specific differences in the distribution of PAVs were seen for each circum-basmati genome: in Basmati 334, for example, chromosome 1 had the lowest density of PAVs, while in Dom Sufid this was the case for chromosome 2 (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S4). On the other hand, both genomes showed significantly higher densities of PAVs on chromosome 10 (Tukeys range test <i>p</i>&lt;0.05). This suggested that, compared to Nipponbare, chromosome 10 was the most differentiated in terms of insertion and deletion variations in both of our circum-basmati genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Evolution of circum-basmati rice group-specific gene presence and absence variation</h3><p>The proportion of repeat sequences found within the larger-sized PAVs (i.e., those &gt;2kb) was high, where between 84 and 98% of large PAVs contained transposable element-related sequences (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S6). Regardless, these larger PAVs also involved loss or gain of coding sequences. For instance, gene ontology analysis of domesticated rice gene orthogroups showed enrichment for genes related to electron transporter activity among both circum-basmati-specific gene losses and gains (see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S7 for gene ontology results for circum-basmati-specific gene losses and Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S8 for gene ontology results for circum-basmati-specific gene gains).</p><p>Many of these genic PAVs could have been important during the rice domestication process [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Meyer RS, Purugganan MD. Evolution of crop species: genetics of domestication and diversification. Nat Rev Genet. 2013;14:84052." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1846">11</a>]. Gene deletions, in particular, are more likely to have a functional consequence than single-nucleotide polymorphisms or short indels and may underlie drastic phenotypic variation. In the context of crop domestication and diversification, this could have led to desirable phenotypes in human-created agricultural environments. For instance, several domestication phenotypes in rice are known to be caused by gene deletions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang M, Yu Y, Haberer G, Marri PR, Fan C, Goicoechea JL, et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat Genet. 2014;46:9828." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1849">35</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Shomura A, Izawa T, Ebana K, Ebitani T, Kanegae H, Konishi S, et al. Deletion in a gene associated with grain size increased yields during rice domestication. Nat Genet. 2008;40:10238." href="#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1852">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhou Y, Zhu J, Li Z, Yi C, Liu J, Zhang H, et al. Deletion in a quantitative trait gene qPE9-1 associated with panicle erectness improves plant architecture during rice domestication. Genetics. 2009;183:31524." href="#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1852_1">63</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lye ZN, Purugganan MD. Copy number variation in domestication. Trends Plant Sci. 2019;24:35265." href="#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1852_2">64</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hu M, Lv S, Wu W, Fu Y, Liu F, Wang B, et al. The domestication of plant architecture in African rice. Plant J. 2018;94:6619." href="#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1852_3">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Wu Y, Zhao S, Li X, Zhang B, Jiang L, Tang Y, et al. Deletions linked to PROG1 gene participate in plant architecture domestication in Asian and African rice. Nat Commun. 2018;9:4157." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1855">66</a>].</p><p>There were 873 gene orthogroups for which neither of the circum-basmati genomes had a gene member, but for which genomes for all three other rice variety groups (N22, Nipponbare, and R498) had at least one gene member. Among these, there were 545 orthogroups for which N22, Nipponbare, and R498 each had a single-copy gene member, suggesting that the deletion of these genes in both the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid genomes could have had a major effect in circum-basmati. We aligned Illumina sequencing data from our circum-basmati population dataset to the japonica Nipponbare genome and calculated deletion frequencies of Nipponbare genes that belonged to the 545 orthogroups (see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S9 for gene deletion frequencies in the circum-basmati population for the Nipponbare genes that are missing in Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid). The vast majority of these Nipponbare genes (509 orthogroups or 93.4%) were entirely absent in the circum-basmati population, further indicating that these were circum-basmati-specific gene deletions fixed within this variety group.</p><p>One of the genes specifically deleted in circum-basmati rice varieties was <i>Awn3-1</i> (Os03g0418600), which was identified in a previous study as associated with altered awn length in japonica rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Li B, Zhang Y, Li J, Yao G, Pan H, Hu G, et al. Fine mapping of two additive effect genes for awn development in rice (Oryza sativa L.). PLoS One. 2016;11:e0160792." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1870">67</a>]. Reduced awn length is an important domestication trait that was selected for ease of harvesting and storing rice seeds [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Hua L, Wang DR, Tan L, Fu Y, Liu F, Xiao L, et al. LABA1, a domestication gene associated with long, barbed awns in wild rice. Plant Cell. 2015;27:187588." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1873">68</a>]. This gene was missing in both circum-basmati genomes, and no region could be aligned to the Nipponbare <i>Awn3-1</i> genic region (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig2">2</a>c). Instead of the <i>Awn3-1</i> coding sequence, this genomic region contained an excess of transposable element sequences, suggesting an accumulation of repetitive DNA may have been involved in this genes deletion. The flanking arms upstream and downstream of Os03g0418600 were annotated in both circum-basmati genomes and were syntenic to the regions in both Nipponbare and N22. These flanking arms, however, were also accumulating transposable element sequences, indicating that this entire genomic region may be degenerating in both circum-basmati rice genomes.</p><p>We then examined the deletion status for other genes involved in the domestication of <i>O. sativa</i>. We focused on the genes that were previously implicated to be involved in the initial domestication phase of rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Meyer RS, Purugganan MD. Evolution of crop species: genetics of domestication and diversification. Nat Rev Genet. 2013;14:84052." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1893">11</a>] where the genes were selected during the transformation of a wild rice into a domesticated ricei.e., <i>Rc</i> (Os07g0211500) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Sweeney MT, Thomson MJ, Pfeil BE, McCouch S. Caught red-handed: Rc encodes a basic helix-loop-helix protein conditioning red pericarp in rice. Plant Cell. 2006;18:28394." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1899">19</a>], <i>Bh4</i> (Os04g0460200) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Zhu B-F, Si L, Wang Z, Zhou Y, Zhu J, Shangguan Y, et al. Genetic control of a transition from black to straw-white seed hull in rice domestication. Plant Physiol. 2011;155:130111." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1906">69</a>], <i>PROG1</i> (Os07g0153600) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Tan L, Li X, Liu F, Sun X, Li C, Zhu Z, et al. Control of a key transition from prostrate to erect growth in rice domestication. Nat Genet. 2008;40:13604." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1912">70</a>], <i>OsC1</i> (Os06g0205100) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Saitoh K, Onishi K, Mikami I, Thidar K, Sano Y. Allelic diversification at the C (OsC1) locus of wild and cultivated rice: nucleotide changes associated with phenotypes. Genetics. 2004;168:9971007." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1918">71</a>], <i>Sh4</i> (Os04g0670900) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Li C, Zhou A, Sang T. Rice domestication by reducing shattering. Science. 2006;311:19369." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1925">72</a>], <i>GS3</i> (Os03g0407400) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Fan C, Xing Y, Mao H, Lu T, Han B, Xu C, et al. GS3, a major QTL for grain length and weight and minor QTL for grain width and thickness in rice, encodes a putative transmembrane protein. Theor Appl Genet. 2006;112:116471." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1931">73</a>], <i>qSH1</i> (Os01g0848400) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Konishi S, Izawa T, Lin SY, Ebana K, Fukuta Y, Sasaki T, et al. An SNP caused loss of seed shattering during rice domestication. Science. 2006;312:13926." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1937">20</a>], and <i>qSW5</i> (Os05g0187500) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Shomura A, Izawa T, Ebana K, Ebitani T, Kanegae H, Konishi S, et al. Deletion in a gene associated with grain size increased yields during rice domestication. Nat Genet. 2008;40:10238." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1944">62</a>]. Our aim was to draw inferences on whether the domestication history of circum-basmati rice may have differed from that of the other rice subpopulations. Results showed none of these genes were deleted in the circum-basmati population (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S8). This suggests that unlike the domestication process of domesticated African rice (<i>O. glaberrima</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Choi JY, Zaidem M, Gutaker R, Dorph K, Singh RK, Purugganan MD. The complex geography of domestication of the African rice Oryza glaberrima. PLoS Genet. 2019;15:e1007414." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1953">74</a>]), gene deletions were not a major contributor during the initial domestication phase of circum-basmati rice. Its likely many of the gene deletions that were selected during the domestication of the circum-basmati rice occurred during the cultivation period [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Meyer RS, Purugganan MD. Evolution of crop species: genetics of domestication and diversification. Nat Rev Genet. 2013;14:84052." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1956">11</a>], when culinary or cultural preferences have selected for unique circum-basmati specific traits.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Repetitive DNA and retrotransposon dynamics in the circum-basmati genomes</h3><p>Repetitive DNA makes up more than 44% of the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid genome assemblies (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Tab2">2</a>). Consistent with genomes of other plant species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Kumar A, Bennetzen JL. Plant retrotransposons. Annu Rev Genet. 1999;33:479532." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1970">75</a>], the repetitive DNA was largely composed of Class I retrotransposons, followed by Class II DNA transposons (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig4">4</a>a). In total, 171.1Mb was annotated as repetitive for Basmati 334 and 169.5Mb for Dom Sufid. The amount of repetitive DNA in the circum-basmati genomes was higher than that in the Nipponbare (160.6Mb) and N22 genomes (152.1Mb), but lower than that in the indica R498 (175.9Mb) and IR8 (176.0Mb) genomes. These differences in the total amount of repetitive DNA were similar to overall genome assembly size differences (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Tab3">3</a>), indicating that variation in repeat DNA accumulation is largely driving genome size differences in rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Zuccolo A, Sebastian A, Talag J, Yu Y, Kim H, Collura K, et al. Transposable element distribution, abundance and role in genome size variation in the genus Oryza. BMC Evol Biol. 2007;7:152." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d14746e1979">76</a>].
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="735" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Repetitive DNA landscape of the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid genomes. <b>a</b> Proportion of repetitive DNA content in the circum-basmati genomes represented by each repeat family. <b>b</b> Distribution of insert times for the <i>gypsy</i> and <i>copia</i> LTR retrotransposons. <b>c</b> Phylogeny of <i>gypsy</i> and <i>copia</i> LTR retrotransposons based on the <i>rve</i> gene. LTR retrotransposons were annotated from the reference genomes of domesticated and wild rice</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We focused our attention on retrotransposons, which made up the majority of the rice repetitive DNA landscape (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig4">4</a>a). Using <i>LTRharvest</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Lerat E. Identifying repeats and transposable elements in sequenced genomes: how to find your way through the dense forest of programs. Heredity. 2010;104:52033." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2025">77</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Hoen DR, Hickey G, Bourque G, Casacuberta J, Cordaux R, Feschotte C, et al. A call for benchmarking transposable element annotation methods. Mob DNA. 2015;6:13." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2028">78</a>], we identified and de novo-annotated LTR retrotransposons in the circum-basmati genomes. <i>LTRharvest</i> annotated 5170 and 5150 candidate LTR retrotransposons in Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid, respectively (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: TablesS10 and S11). Of these, 4180 retrotransposons (80.9% of all candidate LTR retrotransposons) in Basmati 334 and 4228 (82.1%) in Dom Sufid were classified as LTR retrotransposons by <i>RepeatMasker</i>s <i>RepeatClassifer</i> tool (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>). Most LTR retrotransposons were from the <i>gypsy</i> and <i>copia</i> superfamilies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Bennetzen JL. The contributions of retroelements to plant genome organization, function and evolution. Trends Microbiol. 1996;4:34753." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2055">79</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Voytas DF, Ausubel FM. A copia-like transposable element family in Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature. 1988;336:2424." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2058">80</a>], which made up 77.1% (3225 <i>gypsy</i> elements) and 21.9% (915 <i>copia</i> elements) of LTR retrotransposons in the Basmati 334 genome, and 76.4% (3231 <i>gypsy</i> elements) and 22.8% (962 <i>copia</i> elements) of LTR retrotransposons in the Dom Sufid genome, respectively. Comparison of LTR retrotransposon content among reference genomes from different rice variety groups (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S5) revealed that genomes assembled to near completion (i.e., Nipponbare, N22, Basmati 334, Dom Sufid, and indica varieties IR8 and R498, as well as MH63 and ZS97 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang J, Chen L-L, Xing F, Kudrna DA, Yao W, Copetti D, et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2016;113:E516371." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2077">40</a>]) had higher numbers of annotated retrotransposons than genomes generated from short-read sequencing data (GP295-1, circum-aus varieties DJ123 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Schatz MC, Maron LG, Stein JC, Wences A, Gurtowski J, Biggers E, et al. Whole genome de novo assemblies of three divergent strains of rice, Oryza sativa , document novel gene space of aus and indica. Genome Biol. 2014;15:506." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2080">38</a>] and Kasalath [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Sakai H, Kanamori H, Arai-Kichise Y, Shibata-Hatta M, Ebana K, Oono Y, et al. Construction of pseudomolecule sequences of the aus rice cultivar Kasalath for comparative genomics of Asian cultivated rice. DNA Res. 2014;21:397405." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2083">39</a>], and indica variety IR64 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Schatz MC, Maron LG, Stein JC, Wences A, Gurtowski J, Biggers E, et al. Whole genome de novo assemblies of three divergent strains of rice, Oryza sativa , document novel gene space of aus and indica. Genome Biol. 2014;15:506." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2086">38</a>]), suggesting genome assemblies from short-read sequencing data may be missing certain repetitive DNA regions.</p><p>Due to the proliferation mechanism of LTR transposons, the DNA divergence of an LTR sequence can be used to approximate the insertion time for an LTR retrotransposon [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="SanMiguel P, Gaut BS, Tikhonov A, Nakajima Y, Bennetzen JL. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat Genet. 1998;20:435." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2092">81</a>]. Compared to other rice reference genomes, the insertion times for the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid LTR retrotransposons were most similar to those observed for elements in the circum-aus N22 genome (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S5). Within our circum-basmati assemblies, the <i>gypsy</i> superfamily elements had a younger average insertion time (~2.2 million years ago) than elements of the <i>copia</i> superfamily (~2.7 million years ago; Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig4">4</a>b).</p><p>Concentrating on <i>gypsy</i> and <i>copia</i> elements with the <i>rve</i> (integrase; Pfam ID: PF00665) gene, we examined the evolutionary dynamics of these LTR retrotransposons by reconstructing their phylogenetic relationships across reference genomes for the four domesticated rice variety groups (N22, Basmati 334, Dom Sufid, R498, IR8, and Nipponbare), and the two wild rice species (<i>O. nivara</i> and <i>O. rufipogon</i>; Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig4">4</a>c). The retrotransposons grouped into distinct phylogenetic clades, which likely reflect repeats belonging to the same family or subfamily [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Wicker T, Sabot F, Hua-Van A, Bennetzen JL, Capy P, Chalhoub B, et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat Rev Genet. 2007;8:97382." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2129">82</a>]. The majority of phylogenetic clades displayed short external and long internal branches, consistent with rapid recent bursts of transposition observed across various rice LTR retrotransposon families [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Vitte C, Panaud O, Quesneville H. LTR retrotransposons in rice (Oryza sativa, L.): recent burst amplifications followed by rapid DNA loss. BMC Genomics. 2007;8:218." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2132">83</a>].</p><p>The <i>gypsy</i> and <i>copia</i> superfamilies each contained a clade in which the majority of elements originated within <i>O. sativa</i>, and only present among the four domesticated rice variety groups (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig4">4</a>c, single star; see Additionalfiles<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: TablesS12 and S13 for their genome coordinates). Elements in the <i>gypsy</i> superfamily phylogenetic clade had sequence similarity (963 out of the 1837 retrotransposons) to elements of the <i>hopi</i> family [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Panaud O, Vitte C, Hivert J, Muzlak S, Talag J, Brar D, et al. Characterization of transposable elements in the genome of rice (Oryza sativa L.) using representational difference analysis (RDA). Mol Genet Genomics. 2002;268:11321." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2161">84</a>], while elements in the <i>copia</i> superfamily phylogenetic clade had sequence similarity (88 out of the 264) to elements in the <i>osr4</i> family [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="McCarthy EM, Liu J, Lizhi G, McDonald JF. Long terminal repeat retrotransposons of Oryza sativa. Genome biology. 2002;3:RESEARCH0053." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2170">85</a>]. Elements of the <i>hopi</i> family are found in high copy number in genomes of domesticated rice varieties [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Carpentier M-C, Manfroi E, Wei F-J, Wu H-P, Lasserre E, Llauro C, et al. Retrotranspositional landscape of Asian rice revealed by 3000 genomes. Nat Commun. 2019;10. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-018-07974-5&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2177">86</a>] and this amplification has happened recently [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Zhang Q-J, Gao L-Z. Rapid and recent evolution of LTR retrotransposons drives rice genome evolution during the speciation of AA- genome Oryza species. G3. 2017:7(6):187585" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2180">87</a>].</p><p>Several retrotransposon clades were restricted to certain rice variety groups. The <i>gypsy</i> superfamily harbored a phylogenetic clade whose elements were only present in genomes of circum-aus, circum-basmati, and indica varieties (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig4">4</a>c, double star; see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S14 for their genome coordinates), while we observed a clade comprised mostly of circum-basmati-specific elements within the <i>copia</i> superfamily (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig4">4</a>c, triple star; see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S15 for their genome coordinates). Only a few members of the <i>gypsy</i>-like clade had sequence similarity (7 out of 478) to elements of the <i>rire3</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Kumekawa N, Ohtsubo H, Horiuchi T, Ohtsubo E. Identification and characterization of novel retrotransposons of the gypsy type in rice. Mol Gen Genet. 1999;260:593602." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2211">88</a>] and <i>rn215</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Chaparro C, Guyot R, Zuccolo A, Pigu B, Panaud O. RetrOryza: a database of the rice LTR-retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35(Database issue):D6670." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2217">89</a>] families. Members of both families are known to be present in high copy numbers in genomes of domesticated rice varieties, but their abundance differs between the japonica and indica variety groups [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Carpentier M-C, Manfroi E, Wei F-J, Wu H-P, Lasserre E, Llauro C, et al. Retrotranspositional landscape of Asian rice revealed by 3000 genomes. Nat Commun. 2019;10. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-018-07974-5&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2221">86</a>], suggesting a <i>rire3</i>- or <i>rn215</i>-like element expansion in the circum-aus, circum-basmati, and indica genomes. A majority of the circum-basmati-specific <i>copia</i>-like elements had sequence similarity (109 out of 113) to members of the <i>houba</i> family [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Panaud O, Vitte C, Hivert J, Muzlak S, Talag J, Brar D, et al. Characterization of transposable elements in the genome of rice (Oryza sativa L.) using representational difference analysis (RDA). Mol Genet Genomics. 2002;268:11321." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2236">84</a>], which are found in high copy numbers in certain individuals, but in lower frequency across the rice population [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Carpentier M-C, Manfroi E, Wei F-J, Wu H-P, Lasserre E, Llauro C, et al. Retrotranspositional landscape of Asian rice revealed by 3000 genomes. Nat Commun. 2019;10. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-018-07974-5&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2240">86</a>]. This suggests the <i>houba</i> family might have undergone a recent expansion specifically within the circum-basmati genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Phylogenomic analysis on the origins of circum-basmati rice</h3><p>We estimated the phylogenetic relationships within and between variety groups of domesticated Asian rice. Our maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree, based on fourfold degenerate sites from the Nipponbare coding sequences (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>a), showed that each cultivar was monophyletic with respect to its variety group of origin. In addition, the circum-basmati group was sister to japonica rice, while the circum-aus group was sister to indica. Consistent with previous observations, the wild rice <i>O. nivara</i> and <i>O. rufipogon</i> were sister to the circum-aus and japonica rice, respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Choi JY, Platts AE, Fuller DQ, Hsing Y-I, Wing RA, Purugganan MD. The rice paradox: multiple origins but single domestication in Asian rice. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:96979." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2263">14</a>]. While this suggests that each domesticated rice variety group may have had independent wild progenitors of origin, it should be noted that recent hybridization between wild and domesticated rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Wang H, Vieira FG, Crawford JE, Chu C, Nielsen R. Asian wild rice is a hybrid swarm with extensive gene flow and feralization from domesticated rice. Genome Res. 2017;27:102938." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2266">90</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Li L-F, Li Y-L, Jia Y, Caicedo AL, Olsen KM. Signatures of adaptation in the weedy rice genome. Nat Genet. 2017;49:8114." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2270">91</a>] could lead to similar phylogenetic relationships.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="955" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Comparative genomic analysis of circum-basmati rice evolution. The <i>Oryza sativa</i> variety groups are labeled as circum-aus (cA), circum-basmati (cB), indica (I), and japonica (J), and the wild relative is <i>O. rufipogon</i> (R). <b>a</b> Maximum likelihood tree based on fourfold degenerate sites. All nodes had over 95% bootstrap support. <b>b</b> Percentage of genes supporting the topology involving japonica Nipponbare, circum-basmati Basmati 334 (B334) and Dom Sufid (DS), and <i>O. rufipogon</i> after an Approximately Unbiased (AU) test. <b>c</b> Results of ABBA-BABA tests. Shown are median Pattersons D-statistics with 95% confidence intervals determined from a bootstrapping procedure. For each tested topology, the outgroup was always <i>O. barthii</i>. <b>d</b> Percentage of genes supporting the topology involving circum-aus N22, circum-basmati, and indica R498 after an AU test. <b>e</b> Per-chromosome distribution of D-statistics for the trio involving R498, N22, and each circum-basmati genome. Genome-wide D-statistics with 95% bootstrap confidence intervals are indicated by the dark and dotted lines. <b>f</b> Model of admixture events that occurred within domesticated Asian rice. The direction of admixture has been left ambiguous, as the ABBA-BABA test cannot detect the direction of gene flow</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further investigate phylogenetic relationships between circum-basmati and japonica, we examined phylogenetic topologies of each gene involving the trio Basmati 334, Nipponbare, and <i>O. rufipogon</i>. For each gene, we tested which of three possible topologies for a rooted three-species treei.e., [(P1, P2), P3], O, where O is outgroup <i>O. barthii</i> and P1, P2, and P3 are Basmati 334 (or Dom Sufid), Nipponbare, and <i>O. rufipogon</i>, respectivelywere found in the highest proportion. For the trio involving Basmati 334, Nipponbare, and <i>O. rufipogon</i>, there were 7581 genes (or 32.6%), and for the trio involving Dom Sufid, Nipponbare, and <i>O. rufipogon</i>, there were 7690 genes (or 33.1%), which significantly rejected one topology over the other two using an Approximately Unbiased (AU) topology test [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Shimodaira H. An approximately unbiased test of phylogenetic tree selection. Syst Biol. 2002;51:492508." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2331">92</a>]. In both trios, the majority of those genes supported a topology that grouped circum-basmati and Nipponbare as sister to each other (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>b; 3881 [or 51.2%] and 4407 [or 57.3%] genes for Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid, respectively). A lower number of genes (3018 [or 39.8%] and 2508 [or 32.6%] genes for Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid, respectively) supported the topology that placed Nipponbare and <i>O. rufipogon</i> together.</p><p>Our initial topology test suggested that the trio involving Dom Sufid, Nipponbare, and <i>O. rufipogon</i> had a higher proportion of genes supporting the [(circum-basmati, japonica), <i>O. rufipogon</i>] topology compared to the trio involving Basmati 334, Nipponbare, and <i>O. rufipogon</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>b). This suggested within-population variation in the amount of japonica or <i>O. rufipogon</i> ancestry across the circum-basmati genomes due to differences in gene flow. To test for introgression, we employed D-statistics from the ABBA-BABA test [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Green RE, Krause J, Briggs AW, Maricic T, Stenzel U, Kircher M, et al. A draft sequence of the Neandertal genome. Science. 2010;328:71022." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2359">93</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Durand EY, Patterson N, Reich D, Slatkin M. Testing for ancient admixture between closely related populations. Mol Biol Evol. 2011;28:223952." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2362">94</a>]. We conducted ABBA-BABA tests involving the topology [(Basmati 334, Dom Sufid), Nipponbare or <i>O. rufipogon</i>] to examine the differences in introgression between the circum-basmati and japonica or <i>O. rufipogon</i> genomes. The results showed significantly positive D-statistics for the topology [(Basmati 334, Dom Sufid), Nipponbare] (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>c left panel; <i>z</i>-score=8.42 and <i>D</i>=0.270.032), indicating that Dom Sufid shared more alleles with japonica than Basmati 334 did due to a history of more admixture with japonica. The D-statistics involving the topology [(Basmati 334, Dom Sufid), <i>O. rufipogon</i>] were also significantly positive (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>c left panel; <i>z</i>-score=5.57 and <i>D</i>=0.210.038).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Signatures of admixture between circum-basmati and circum-aus rice genomes</h3><p>Due to extensive admixture between rice variety group genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Choi JY, Platts AE, Fuller DQ, Hsing Y-I, Wing RA, Purugganan MD. The rice paradox: multiple origins but single domestication in Asian rice. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:96979." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2401">14</a>], we examined whether the basmati genome was also influenced by gene flow with other divergent rice variety groups (i.e., circum-aus or indica rice). A topology test was conducted for a rooted, three-population species tree. For the trio involving Basmati 334, circum-aus variety N22, and indica variety R498, there were 7859 genes (or 35.3%), and for the trio involving Dom Sufid, N22, and R498, there were 8109 genes (or 37.8%), which significantly rejected one topology over the other two after an AU test. In both trios, more than half of the genes supported the topology grouping circum-aus and indica as sisters (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>d). In addition, more genes supported the topology grouping circum-aus and circum-basmati as sisters than the topology grouping indica and circum-basmati as sisters. This suggested that the circum-aus variety group might have contributed a larger proportion of genes to circum-basmati through gene flow than the indica variety group did.</p><p>To test for evidence of admixture, we conducted ABBA-BABA tests involving trios of the circum-basmati, N22, and R498 genomes. Results showed significant evidence of gene flow between circum-aus and both circum-basmati genomesFig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>c, right panel; <i>z</i>-score=5.70 and <i>D</i>=0.0820.014 for topology [(R498, N22), Basmati 334]; and <i>z</i>-score=8.44 and <i>D</i>=0.110.013 for topology [(R498, N22), Dom Sufid]. To test whether there was variability in the circum-aus or indica ancestry in each of the circum-basmati genomes, we conducted ABBA-BABA tests for the topology [(Basmati 334, Dom Sufid), N22 or R498]. Neither of the ABBA-BABA tests involving the topology [(Basmati 334, Dom Sufid), N22] (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>c, right panel; <i>z</i>-score=1.20 and <i>D</i>=0.0250.021) or the topology [(Basmati 334, Dom Sufid), R498] (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>c, right panel; <i>z</i>-score=2.24 and <i>D</i>=0.060.026) was significant, suggesting the amount of admixture from circum-aus to each of the two circum-basmati genomes was similar.</p><p>Because of the significant amount of admixture occurring between the circum-aus and circum-basmatigenomes, we examined whether this had affected the topology analysis involving the trio japonica, circum-basmati, and <i>O. rufipogon</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>b). Specifically, we assessed whether the grouping of japonica and <i>O. rufipogon</i> as sister species (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>a) was an evolutionary artifact due to sharing of alleles between circum-basmati and circum-aus through admixture. We examined this by conducting the AU test on the four populations involving circum-aus, circum-basmati (Basmati 334 or Dom Sufid), japonica, and <i>O. rufipogon</i>, testing which of the 15 possible topologies for a rooted four-population sample (see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S6 for the 15 topologies tested) was the best fit for each gene. Results showed there were 2774 genes involving Basmati 334 and 2665 genes involving Dom Sufid where the AU test significantly rejected one topology over the other 14 topologies (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S6). The most frequent topology (&gt;30% of the genes) was one that both grouped japonica and <i>O. rufipogon</i> as sisters and grouped circum-basmati and circum-aus as sisters, which is a topology that occurs when there is admixture occurring between circum-basmati and circum-aus. The second most frequent topology (&gt;20% of the genes) was the species phylogeny (i.e., [(circum-basmati, japonica), <i>O. rufipogon</i>]) and this was represented fivefold higher than the remaining 13 topologies. In the end, this result partially explains the discrepancy between the genome-wide tree topology (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>a) and the gene-specific tree topology (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>b). The admixture occurring between circum-basmati and circum-aus had led to the spurious genome-wide topological relationship.</p><p>In sum, the phylogenomic analysis indicated that circum-basmati and japonica share the most recent common ancestor, while circum-aus has admixed with circum-basmati during its evolutionary history (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>f). We then examined whether admixture from circum-aus had affected each of the circum-basmati chromosomes to a similar degree. For both circum-basmati genomes, most chromosomes had D-statistics that were not different from the genome-wide D-statistics value or from zero (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>e). Exceptions were chromosomes 10 and 11, where the bootstrap D-statistics were significantly higher than the genome-wide estimate.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Population analysis on the origin of circum-basmati rice</h3><p>Since our analysis was based on single representative genomes from each rice variety group, we compared the results of our phylogenomic analyses to population genomic patterns in an expanded set of rice varieties from different groups. We obtained high coverage (&gt;14) genomic re-sequencing data (generated with Illumina short-read sequencing) from landrace varieties in the 3K Rice Genome Project [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557:439." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2498">7</a>] and from circum-basmati rice landraces we re-sequenced. In total, we analyzed 24 circum-aus, 18 circum-basmati, and 37 tropical japonica landraces (see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S16 for variety names). The raw Illumina sequencing reads were aligned to the scaffolded Basmati 334 genome and computationally genotyped. A total of 4,594,290 polymorphic sites were called across the three rice variety groups and used for further analysis.</p><p>To quantify relationships between circum-aus, circum-basmati, and japonica, we conducted a topology-weighting analysis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Martin SH, Van Belleghem SM. Exploring evolutionary relationships across the genome using topology weighting. Genetics. 2017;206:42938." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2507">95</a>]. For three populations, there are three possible topologies and we conducted localized sliding window analysis to quantify the number of unique sub-trees that supported each tree topology. Consistent with the phylogenomic analysis results, the topology weight was the largest for the topology that grouped japonica and circum-basmati as sisters (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig6">6</a>a; topology weight=0.481 with 95% confidence interval [0.4790.483]). The topology that grouped circum-aus and circum-basmati together as sisters weighed significantly more (topology weight=0.318 with 95% confidence interval [0.3160.320]) than the topology that grouped japonica and circum-aus as sisters (topology weight=0.201 with 95% confidence interval [0.1990.203]). This was consistent with the admixture results from the comparative phylogenomic analysis, which detected evidence of gene flow between circum-aus and circum-basmati.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1126" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Population relationships among the circum-aus (cA), circum-basmati (cB), and japonica rice (J). <b>a</b> Sum of genome-wide topology weights for a three-population topology involving trios of the circum-aus, circum-basmati, and japonica rice. Topology weights were estimated across windows with 100 SNPs. <b>b</b> Chromosomal distributions of topology weights involving trios of the circum-aus, circum-basmati, and japonica rice (left), and the sum of the topology weights (right). <b>c</b> Best-fitting <i>ai</i> model for the circum-aus, circum-basmati, and japonica rice. See Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S17 for parameter estimates</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A treemix analysis was conducted for the three domesticated rice population (circum-aus, circum-basmati, and japonica) alongside the wild rice <i>O. rufipogon</i> and <i>O. barthii</i> (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S7). We fitted zero to three migration edges in the model, and at three migration edges, the model log-likelihood started plateauing (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S7B). At three migration edges, a migration edge was fitted between circum-aus and circum-basmati (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S7A), consistent with our previous results. In addition, there were migration edges fitted between the wild rice <i>O. rufipogon</i> and circum-basmati and between the wild rice <i>O. barthii</i> and japonica. Overall, these migration results were consistent with recent studies that have documented the occurrence of admixture between wild and domesticated rice populations [74, 90, 91].</p><p>We then examined topology weights for each individual chromosome, since the ABBA-BABA tests using the genome assemblies had detected variation in circum-aus ancestry between different chromosomes (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig5">5</a>e). The results showed that for most of the chromosomes the topology [(japonica, circum-basmati), circum-aus] always weighed more than the remaining two topologies. An exception was observed for chromosome 10 where the topology weight grouping circum-aus and circum-basmati as sisters was significantly higher (topology weight=0.433 with 95% confidence interval [0.4240.442]) than the weight for the genome-wide topology that grouped japonica and circum-basmati as sisters (topology weight=0.320 with 95% confidence interval [0.3120.328]). This change in predominant topology was still observed when the weights were calculated across wider local windows (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S8). Another exception could be seen for chromosome 6 where the genome-wide topology [(japonica, circum-basmati), circum-aus] (topology weight=0.367 with 95% confidence interval [0.3590.374]) and the admixture topology [(circum-aus, circum-basmati), japonica] (topology weight=0.355 with 95% confidence interval [0.3490.362]) had almost equal weights. In larger window sizes, the weight of the admixed topology was slightly higher than that of the genome-wide topology (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S8).</p><p>To estimate the evolutionary/domestication scenario that might explain the observed relationships between the circum-aus, circum-basmati, and japonica groups, we used the diffusion-based approach of the program <i>ai</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Gutenkunst RN, Hernandez RD, Williamson SH, Bustamante CD. Inferring the joint demographic history of multiple populations from multidimensional SNP frequency data. PLoS Genet. 2009;5:e1000695." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2580">96</a>] and fitted specific demographic models to the observed allele frequency spectra for the three rice variety groups. Because all three rice groups have evidence of admixture with each other [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557:439." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2583">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="He Z, Zhai W, Wen H, Tang T, Wang Y, Lu X, et al. Two evolutionary histories in the genome of rice: the roles of domestication genes. PLoS Genet. 2011;7:e1002100." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2586">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Choi JY, Platts AE, Fuller DQ, Hsing Y-I, Wing RA, Purugganan MD. The rice paradox: multiple origins but single domestication in Asian rice. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:96979." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2589">14</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Huang X, Kurata N, Wei X, Wang Z-X, Wang A, Zhao Q, et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature. 2012;490:497501." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2593">16</a>], we examined 13 demographic scenarios involving symmetric, asymmetric, and no migration models between variety groups, with and without recent population size changes (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S9). To minimize the effect of genetic linkage on the demography estimation, polymorphic sites were randomly pruned in 200-kb windows, resulting in 1918 segregating sites. The best-fitting demographic scenario was one that modeled a period of lineage splitting and isolation, while gene flow only occurred after formation of the three populations and at a later time (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig6">6</a>c; visualizations of the 2D site frequency spectrum and model fit can be seen in Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S10). This best-fitting model was one of the lesser-parameterized models we tested, and the difference in Akaike Information Criterion (AIC) with the model with the second-highest likelihood was 25.46 (see Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S17 for parameter estimates and maximum likelihood estimates for each demographic model).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genetic structure within the circum-basmati group</h3><p>We used the circum-basmati population genomic data for the 78 varieties aligned to the scaffolded Basmati 334 genome and called the polymorphic sites segregating within this variety group. After filtering, a total of 4,430,322 SNPs across the circum-basmati dataset remained, which were used to examine population genetic relationships within circum-basmati.</p><p>We conducted principal component analysis (PCA) using the polymorphism data and color-coded each circum-basmati rice variety according to its country of origin (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig7">7</a>a). The PCA suggested that circum-basmati rice could be divided into three major groups with clear geographic associations: (group 1) a largely Bhutan/Nepal-based group, (group 2) an India/Bangladesh/Myanmar-based group, and (group 3) an Iran/Pakistan-based group. The rice varieties that could not be grouped occupied an ambiguous space across the principal components, suggesting these might represent admixed rice varieties.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Fig. 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-020-1938-2/MediaObjects/13059_2020_1938_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="497" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Population structure within the circum-basmati rice. <b>a</b> PCA plot for the 78-variety circum-basmati rice population genomic dataset. The three genetic groups designated by this study can be seen in the color-coded circles with dashed lines. <b>b</b> Proportion of ancestry plot for <i>K</i>=2, 3, 4, and 5 across the 78 circum-basmati rice varieties. The color-coding from (<b>a</b>) is indicated above each samples ancestry proportion. <b>c</b> Geographic distribution of the 78 circum-basmati rice varieties with their grouping status color-coded according to <b>a</b>. <b>d</b> Agronomic measurements for the 78 circum-basmati rice varieties sorted into the three groups designated by this study. Two asterisks indicate <i>p</i> value &lt;0.01 and three asterisks indicate <i>p</i> value &lt;0.001</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To obtain better insight into the ancestry of each rice variety, we used <i>fastSTRUCTURE</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Raj A, Stephens M, Pritchard JK. fastSTRUCTURE: variational inference of population structure in large SNP data sets. Genetics. 2014;197:57389." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2665">97</a>] and varied assumed ancestral population (<i>K</i>) from 2 to 5 groups so the ancestry proportion of each rice variety could be estimated (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig7">7</a>b). At <i>K</i>=2, the India/Bangladesh/Myanmar and Iran/Pakistan rice groups were shown to have distinct ancestral components, while the Bhutan/Nepal group was largely an admixture of the other two groups. At <i>K</i>=3, the grouping status designated from the PCA was largely concordant with the ancestral components. At <i>K</i>=4, most India/Bangladesh/Myanmar rice had a single ancestral component, but Iran/Pakistan rice had two ancestral components that were shared with several Bhutan/Nepal landraces. Furthermore, several of the cultivars from the latter group seemed to form an admixed group with India/Bangladesh/Myanmar varieties. In fact, when a phylogenetic tree was reconstructed using the polymorphic sites, varieties within the India/Bangladesh/Myanmar and Iran/Pakistan groups formed a monophyletic clade with each other. On the other hand, Bhutan/Nepal varieties formed a paraphyletic group where several clustered with the Iran/Pakistan varieties (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S11).</p><p>We then conducted a second <i>fastSTRUCTURE</i> analysis on the circum-basmati population, this time including the japonica and circum-aus populations while varying <i>K</i> from 2 to 5 groups (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S12). From <i>K</i>=2 to 5, the japonica and circum-aus groups always formed two distinct genetic groups. At <i>K</i>=5, the three circum<i>-</i>basmati genetic groups that were designated in the first analysis (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig7">7</a>) were still observed across the circum-basmati population. In lower <i>K</i>, we see that the different circum-basmati genetic groups had differing amounts of japonica or circum-aus ancestry. Specifically, the Iran/Pakistan group had more circum-aus ancestry, while the India/Bangladesh/Myanmar group had more japonica ancestry. The Bhutan/Nepal group again was suggested to be an admixture of the other two circum-basmati rice genetic groups.</p><p>In summary, the circum-basmati rice has evolved across a geographic gradient with at least three genetic groups (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig7">7</a>c). These existed as distinct ancestral groups that later admixed to form several other circum-basmati varieties. Group 1 and group 3 rice in particular may have experienced greater admixture, while the group 2 landraces remained genetically more isolated from other circum-basmati subpopulations. We also found differences in agronomic traits associated with our designated groups (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Fig7">7</a>d). The grain length to width ratio, which is a highly prized trait in certain circum-basmati rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Singh R, Singh U, Khush G. Aromatic rices. New Delhi: Oxford &amp; IBH Publishing Co Pvt Ltd; 2000." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2725">24</a>], was significantly larger in group 3 Iran/Pakistan varieties. The thousand-kernel weights, on the other hand, were highest for group 2 India/Bangladesh/Myanmar varieties and were significantly higher than those for the ungrouped and group 1 Bhutan/Nepal varieties.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><p>Nanopore sequencing is becoming an increasingly popular approach to sequence and assemble the often large and complex genomes of plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Michael TP, Jupe F, Bemm F, Motley ST, Sandoval JP, Lanz C, et al. High contiguity Arabidopsis thaliana genome assembly with a single nanopore flow cell. Nat Commun. 2018;9:541." href="#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2737">98</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schmidt MH, Vogel A, Denton AK, Istace B, Wormit A, van de Geest H, et al. De novo assembly of a new Solanum pennellii accession using nanopore sequencing. Plant Cell. 2017;29(10):233648." href="#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2737_1">99</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Belser C, Istace B, Denis E, Dubarry M, Baurens F-C, Falentin C, et al. Chromosome-scale assemblies of plant genomes using nanopore long reads and optical maps. Nature Plants. 2018;4:879." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2740">100</a>]. Here, using long-read sequences generated with Oxford Nanopore Technologies sequencing platform, we assembled genomes of two circum-basmati rice cultivars, with quality metrics that were comparable to other rice variety group reference genome assemblies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2743">37</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang J, Chen L-L, Xing F, Kudrna DA, Yao W, Copetti D, et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2016;113:E516371." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2746">40</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Du H, Yu Y, Ma Y, Gao Q, Cao Y, Chen Z, et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:15324." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2749">41</a>]. With modest genome coverage, we were able to develop reference genome assemblies that represented a significant improvement over a previous circum-basmati reference genome sequence, which had been assembled with a&gt;3-fold higher genome coverage than ours, but from short-read sequences [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhao Q, Feng Q, Lu H, Li Y, Wang A, Tian Q, et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat Genet. 2018;50:27884." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2753">42</a>]. With additional short-read sequencing reads, we were able to correct errors from the nanopore sequencing reads, resulting in two high-quality circum-basmati genome assemblies.</p><p>Even with long-read sequence data, developing good plant reference genome sequences still requires additional technologies such as optical mapping or Hi-C sequencing for improving assembly contiguity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Howe K, Wood JM. Using optical mapping data for the improvement of vertebrate genome assemblies. GigaScience. 2015;4:10." href="#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2759">101</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Goodwin S, McPherson JD, McCombie WR. Coming of age: ten years of next-generation sequencing technologies. Nat Rev Genet. 2016;17:33351." href="#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2759_1">102</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Udall JA, Dawe RK. Is it ordered correctly? Validating genome assemblies by optical mapping. Plant Cell. 2018;30:714." href="#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2759_2">103</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Sedlazeck FJ, Lee H, Darby CA, Schatz MC. Piercing the dark matter: bioinformatics of long-range sequencing and mapping. Nat Rev Genet. 2018;19:32946." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2762">104</a>], which can be error prone as well [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Alonge M, Soyk S, Ramakrishnan S, Wang X, Goodwin S, Sedlazeck FJ, et al. RaGOO: fast and accurate reference-guided scaffolding of draft genomes. Genome Biol. 2019;20:224." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2765">56</a>]. Our assemblies were also fragmented into multiple contigs, but sizes of these contigs were sufficiently large that we could use reference genome sequences from another rice variety group to anchor the majority of contigs and scaffold them to higher-order chromosome-level assemblies.
