Hence, with a highly contiguous draft genome assembly, reference genome-based scaffolding can be a cost-efficient and powerful method of generating chromosome-level assemblies.</p><p>Repetitive DNA constitutes large proportions of plant genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Wendel JF, Jackson SA, Meyers BC, Wing RA. Evolution of plant genome architecture. Genome Biol. 2016;17:37." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2771">105</a>], and there is an advantage to using long-read sequences for genome assembly as it enables better annotation of transposable elements. Many transposable element insertions have evolutionarily deleterious consequences in the rice genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Choi JY, Purugganan MD. Evolutionary epigenomics of retrotransposon-mediated methylation spreading in rice. Mol Biol Evol. 2018;35:36582." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2774">54</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="vonHoldt BM, Takuno S, Gaut BS. Recent retrotransposon insertions are methylated and phylogenetically clustered in japonica rice (Oryza sativa spp. japonica). Mol Biol Evol. 2012;29:3193203." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2777">106</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Baucom RS, Estill JC, Leebens-Mack J, Bennetzen JL. Natural selection on gene function drives the evolution of LTR retrotransposon families in the rice genome. Genome Res. 2009;19:24354." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2780">107</a>], but some insertions could have beneficial effects on the host [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Naito K, Zhang F, Tsukiyama T, Saito H, Hancock CN, Richardson AO, et al. Unexpected consequences of a sudden and massive transposon amplification on rice gene expression. Nature. 2009;461:11304." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2783">108</a>]. Using our genome assembly, we have identified retrotransposon families that have expanded specifically within circum-basmati genomes. While more study will be necessary to understand the functional effects of these insertions, long-read sequences have greatly improved the assembly and identification of repeat types.</p><p>Due to a lack of archaeobotanical data, the origins of circum-basmati rice have remained elusive. Studies of this variety groups origins have primarily focused on genetic differences that exist between circum-basmati and other Asian rice variety groups [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Garris AJ, Tai TH, Coburn J, Kresovich S, McCouch S. Genetic structure and diversity in Oryza sativa L. Genetics. 2005;169:16318." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2789">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557:439." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2792">7</a>]. Recently, a study suggested that circum-basmati rice (called aromatic in that study) was a product of hybridization between the circum-aus and japonica rice variety groups [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Civ P, Craig H, Cox CJ, Brown TA. Three geographically separate domestications of Asian rice. Nat Plants. 2015;1:15164." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2795">17</a>]. This inference was based on observations of phylogenetic relationships across genomic regions that showed evidence of domestication-related selective sweeps. These regions mostly grouped circum-basmati with japonica or circum-aus. In addition, chloroplast haplotype analysis indicated that most circum-basmati varieties carried a chloroplast derived from a wild rice most closely related to circum-aus landraces [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Civ P, Ali S, Batista-Navarro R, Drosou K, Ihejieto C, Chakraborty D, et al. Origin of the aromatic group of cultivated rice (Oryza sativa L.) traced to the Indian subcontinent. Genome Biol Evol. 2019;11:83243." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2798">109</a>]. Our evolutionary analysis of circum-basmati rice genomes generally supported this view. Although our results suggest that circum-basmati had its origins primarily in japonica, we also find significant evidence of gene flow originating from circum-aus, which we detected both in comparative genomic and population genomic analyses. Our results are also consistent with the observation of genome-wide fine-scale admixture tracts that showed japonica rice contributing the highest amount of genetic material to circum-basmati, followed by circum-aus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Santos JD, Chebotarov D, McNally KL, Bartholom J, Droc G, Billot C, et al. Fine scale genomic signals of admixture and alien introgression among Asian rice landraces. Genome Biol Evol. 2019;11:135873." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2801">110</a>]. Demographic modeling indicated a period of isolation among circum-aus, circum-basmati, and japonica, with gene flow occurring only after lineage splitting of each group. Here, our model is consistent with the current view that gene flow is a key evolutionary process associated with the diversification of rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Fuller DQ. Pathways to Asian civilizations: tracing the origins and spread of rice and rice cultures. Rice. 2012;4:7892." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2805">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang X, Han B. Rice domestication occurred through single origin and multiple introgressions. Nat Plants. 2015;2:15207." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2808">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Castillo CC, Tanaka K, Sato Y-I, Ishikawa R, Bellina B, Higham C, et al. Archaeogenetic study of prehistoric rice remains from Thailand and India: evidence of early japonica in South and Southeast Asia. Archaeol Anthropol Sci. 2016;8:52343." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2808_1">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Choi JY, Platts AE, Fuller DQ, Hsing Y-I, Wing RA, Purugganan MD. The rice paradox: multiple origins but single domestication in Asian rice. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:96979." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2811">14</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Huang X, Kurata N, Wei X, Wang Z-X, Wang A, Zhao Q, et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature. 2012;490:497501." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2814">16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Molina J, Sikora M, Garud N, Flowers JM, Rubinstein S, Reynolds A, et al. Molecular evidence for a single evolutionary origin of domesticated rice. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2011;108:83516." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2817">111</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Fuller DQ. Finding plant domestication in the Indian subcontinent. Curr Anthropol. 2011;52:S34762." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2820">112</a>].</p><p>Interestingly, we found that chromosome 10 of circum-basmati had an evolutionary history that differed significantly from that of other chromosomes. Specifically, compared to japonica, this chromosome had the highest proportion of presence/absence variation and shared more alleles with circum-aus. Based on this result, we hypothesize that this is largely due to higher levels of introgression from circum-aus into chromosome 10 compared to other chromosomes. Such a deviation of evolutionary patterns on a single chromosome has been observed in the <i>Aquilegia</i> genus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="Filiault DL, Ballerini ES, Mandkov T, Akz G, Derieg NJ, Schmutz J, et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. eLife. 2018;7:e36426." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR113" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2830">113</a>], but to our knowledge has not been observed elsewhere. Why this occurred is unclear at present, but it may be that selection has driven a higher proportion of circum-aus alleles into chromosome 10. Future work will be necessary to clarify the consequence of this higher level of admixture on chromosome 10.</p><p>Very little is known about population genomic diversity within circum-basmati. Our analysis suggests the existence of at least three genetic groups within this variety group, and these groups showed geographic structuring. Several varieties from group 1 (Bhutan/Nepal) and group 3 (Iran/Pakistan) had population genomic signatures consistent with an admixed population, while group 2 (India/Bangladesh/Myanmar) was genetically more distinct from the other two subpopulations. In addition, the geographic location of the India/Bangladesh/Myanmar group largely overlaps the region where circum-aus varieties were historically grown [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 114" title="Liakat Ali M, McClung AM, Jia MH, Kimball JA, McCouch SR, Georgia CE. A rice diversity panel evaluated for genetic and agro-morphological diversity between subpopulations and its geographic distribution. Crop Sci. 2011;51:202135." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR114" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2836">114</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 115" title="Travis AJ, Norton GJ, Datta S, Sarma R, Dasgupta T, Savio FL, et al. Assessing the genetic diversity of rice originating from Bangladesh, Assam and West Bengal. Rice (N Y). 2015;8:35." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR115" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2839">115</a>]. Our genome-wide evidence of geographic structuring is also consistent with past studies that examined isozyme loci or simple sequence repeat (SSR) markers in Asian rice, and finding a longitudinal genetic structure from Iran to Myanmar. Isozymes could differentiate the circum-basmati rice from other Asian rice subpopulations (classified as group V in Glaszmann [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Glaszmann JC. Isozymes and classification of Asian rice varieties. Theoret Appl Genetics. 1987;74:2130." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2842">8</a>]) and the varieties from Iran were especially differentiated at two isozyme loci [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 116" title="Glaszmann JC. Geographic pattern of variation among Asian native rice cultivars (Oryza sativa L.) based on fifteen isozyme loci. Genome. 1988;30:78292." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR116" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2845">116</a>]. SSR markers differentiated the Myanmar group V varieties from the rest of south and west Asian varieties [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 117" title="Myint KM, Courtois B, Risterucci A-M, Frouin J, Soe K, Thet KM, et al. Specific patterns of genetic diversity among aromatic rice varieties in Myanmar. Rice (N Y). 2012;5. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1939-8433-5-20&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR117" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2848">117</a>]. Based on this study and past evidences, genome-wide survey of an expanded sample of circum-basmati should assist in determining the fine-scale genetic structure of the circum-basmati population.</p><p>Given the extensive history of admixture that circum-basmati rice has with circum-aus, the India/Bangladesh/Myanmar group may have been influenced particularly strongly by gene flow from circum-aus. How these three genetic subpopulations were established may require a deeper sampling with in-depth analysis, but the geographically structured genomic variation shows that the diversity of circum-basmati has clearly been underappreciated. In addition, the Basmati 334 and Dom Sufid varieties, for which we generated genome assemblies in this study, both belong to the Iran/Pakistan genetic group. Thus, our study still leaves a gap in our knowledge of genomic variation in the Bhutan/Nepal and India/Bangladesh/Myanmar genetic groups, and varieties in these groups would be obvious next targets for generating additional genome assemblies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><p>In conclusion, our study shows that generating high-quality plant genome assemblies is feasible with relatively modest amounts of resources and data. Using nanopore sequencing, we were able to produce contiguous, chromosome-level genome assemblies for cultivars in a rice variety group that contains economically and culturally important varieties. Our reference genome sequences have the potential to be important genomic resources for identifying single-nucleotide polymorphisms and larger structural variations that are unique to circum-basmati rice. Analyzing de novo genome assemblies for a larger sample of Asian rice will be important for uncovering and studying hidden population genomic variation too complex to study with only short-read sequencing technology.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec16" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec16-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec16">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec16-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Plant material</h3><p>Basmati 334 (IRGC 27819; GeneSys passport: <a href="https://purl.org/germplasm/id/23601903-f8c3-4642-a7fc-516a5bc154f7">https://purl.org/germplasm/id/23601903-f8c3-4642-a7fc-516a5bc154f7</a>) is a basmati (sensu stricto) landrace from Pakistan and was originally donated to the International Rice Research Institute (IRRI) by the Agricultural Research Council (ARC) in Karachi (donor accession ID: PAK. SR. NO. 39). Dom Sufid (IRGC 117265; GeneSys passport: <a href="https://purl.org/germplasm/id/fb861458-09de-46c4-b9ca-f5c439822919">https://purl.org/germplasm/id/fb861458-09de-46c4-b9ca-f5c439822919</a>) is a sadri landrace from Iran. Seeds from accessions IRGC 27819 and IRGC 117265 were obtained from the IRRI seed bank, surface-sterilized with bleach, and germinated in the dark on a wet paper towel for 4days. Seedlings were transplanted individually in pots containing continuously wet soil in a greenhouse at New York Universitys Center for Genomics and Systems Biology and cultivated under a 12-hday-12-h night photoperiod at 30C. Plants were kept in the dark in a growth cabinet under the same climatic conditions for 4days prior to tissue harvesting. Continuous darkness induced chloroplast degradation, which diminishes the amount of chloroplast DNA that would otherwise end up in the DNA extracted from the leaves.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">DNA extractions</h3><p>Thirty-six 100-mg samples (3.6g total) of leaf tissue from a total of 10 one-month-old plants were flash-frozen at harvest for each accession and stored at 80C. DNA extractions were performed by isolating the cell nuclei and gently lysing the nuclei to extract intact DNA molecules [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 118" title="Zhang H-B, Zhao X, Ding X, Paterson AH, Wing RA. Preparation of megabase-size DNA from plant nuclei. Plant J. 1995;7:17584." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR118" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2890">118</a>]. Yields ranged between 140 and 150ng/l.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Library preparation and nanopore sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was visualized on an agarose gel to determine shearing. DNA was size-selected using BluePippin BLF7510 cassette (Sage Science) and high-pass mode (&gt;20kb) and prepared using Oxford Nanopore Technologies standard ligation sequencing kit SQK-LSK109. FLO-MIN106 (R9.4) flowcells were used for sequencing on the GridION X5 platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Library preparation and Illumina sequencing</h3><p>Extracted genomic DNA was prepared for short-read sequencing using the Illumina Nextera DNA Library Preparation Kit. Sequencing was done on the Illumina HiSeq 2500  HighOutput Mode v3 with 2100bp read configuration, at the New York University Genomics Core Facility.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Genome assembly, polishing, and scaffolding</h3><p>After completion of sequencing, the raw signal intensity data was used for base calling using <i>flip flop</i> (version 2.3.5) from Oxford Nanopore Technologies. Reads with a mean qscore (quality) greater than 8 and a read length greater than 8kb were used and trimmed for adaptor sequences using <i>Porechop</i> (<a href="https://github.com/rrwick/Porechop">https://github.com/rrwick/Porechop</a>). Raw nanopore sequencing reads were corrected using the program <i>Canu</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="Koren S, Walenz BP, Berlin K, Miller JR, Bergman NH, Phillippy AM. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 2017;27:72236." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2931">119</a>] and then assembled with the genome assembler <i>Flye</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 120" title="Kolmogorov M, Yuan J, Lin Y, Pevzner PA. Assembly of long, error-prone reads using repeat graphs. Nat Biotechnol. 2019;37:540." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR120" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2938">120</a>].</p><p>The initial draft assemblies were polished for three rounds using the raw nanopore reads with <i>Racon</i> ver. 1.2.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 121" title="Vaser R, Sovi I, Nagarajan N, iki M. Fast and accurate de novo genome assembly from long uncorrected reads. Genome Res. 2017;27:73746." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR121" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2947">121</a>] and one round with <i>Medaka</i> (<a href="https://github.com/nanoporetech/medaka">https://github.com/nanoporetech/medaka</a>) from Oxford Nanopore Technologies. Afterwards, reads from Illumina sequencing were used by <i>bwa-mem</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 122" title="Li H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. arXiv. 2013:1303.3997v2." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR122" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2961">122</a>] to align to the draft genome assemblies. The alignment files were then used by <i>Pilon</i> ver. 1.22 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 123" title="Walker BJ, Abeel T, Shea T, Priest M, Abouelliel A, Sakthikumar S, et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One. 2014;9:e112963." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR123" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2967">123</a>] for three rounds of polishing.</p><p>Contigs were scaffolded using a reference genome-guided scaffolding approach implemented in <i>RaGOO</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Alonge M, Soyk S, Ramakrishnan S, Wang X, Goodwin S, Sedlazeck FJ, et al. RaGOO: fast and accurate reference-guided scaffolding of draft genomes. Genome Biol. 2019;20:224." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2976">56</a>]. Using the Nipponbare genome as a reference, we aligned the circum-basmati genomes using <i>Minimap2</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 124" title="Li H. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics. 2018;34:3094100." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR124" id="ref-link-section-d14746e2982">124</a>]. <i>RaGOO</i> was then used to order the assembly contigs. Space between contigs was artificially filled in with 100 N blocks.</p><p>Genome assembly statistics were calculated using the <i>bbmap stats.sh</i> script from the <i>BBTools</i> suite (<a href="https://jgi.doe.gov/data-and-tools/bbtools/">https://jgi.doe.gov/data-and-tools/bbtools/</a>). Completeness of the genome assemblies was evaluated using <i>BUSCO</i> ver. 2.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 125" title="Simo FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:32102." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR125" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3004">125</a>]. Synteny between the circum-basmati genomes and the Nipponbare genome was visualized using <i>D-GENIES</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 126" title="Cabanettes F, Klopp C. D-GENIES: dot plot large genomes in an interactive, efficient and simple way. PeerJ. 2018;6. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.7717/peerj.4958&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR126" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3011">126</a>]. Genome-wide dotplot from <i>D-GENIES</i> indicated the initial genome assembly of Dom Sufid had an evidence of a large chromosomal fusion between the ends of chromosome 4 and 10. Closer examination of this contig (named contig_28 of Dom Sufid) showed the break point overlapped the telomeric repeat sequence, indicating there had been a misassembly between the ends of chromosome 4 and 10. Hence, contig_28 was broken up into two so that each contig represented the respective chromosome of origin and was then subsequently scaffolded using <i>RaGOO</i>.</p><p>Inversions that were observed in the dot plot were computationally verified independently using raw nanopore reads. The long-read-aware aligner <i>ngmlr</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Sedlazeck FJ, Rescheneder P, Smolka M, Fang H, Nattestad M, von Haeseler A, et al. Accurate detection of complex structural variations using single-molecule sequencing. Nat Methods. 2018;15:4618." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3027">55</a>] was used to align the nanopore reads to the Nipponbare genome, after which the long-read-aware structural variation caller <i>sniffles</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Sedlazeck FJ, Rescheneder P, Smolka M, Fang H, Nattestad M, von Haeseler A, et al. Accurate detection of complex structural variations using single-molecule sequencing. Nat Methods. 2018;15:4618." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3033">55</a>] was used to call and detect inversions.</p><p>The number of sites aligning to the Nipponbare genome was determined using the <i>Mummer4</i> package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 127" title="Marais G, Delcher AL, Phillippy AM, Coston R, Salzberg SL, Zimin A. MUMmer4: a fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput Biol. 2018;14:e1005944." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR127" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3042">127</a>]. Alignment delta files were analyzed with the <i>dnadiff</i> suite from the <i>Mummer4</i> package to calculate the number of aligned sites and the number of differences between the Nipponbare genome and the circum-basmati genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Gene annotation and analysis</h3><p>Gene annotation was conducted using the <i>MAKER</i> program [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Cantarel BL, Korf I, Robb SMC, Parra G, Ross E, Moore B, et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 2008;18:18896." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3062">52</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Holt C, Yandell M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:491." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3065">53</a>]. An in-depth description of running <i>MAKER</i> can be found on the website: <a href="https://gist.github.com/darencard/bb1001ac1532dd4225b030cf0cd61ce2">https://gist.github.com/darencard/bb1001ac1532dd4225b030cf0cd61ce2</a>. We used published <i>Oryza</i> genic sequences as evidence for the gene modeling process. We downloaded the Nipponbare cDNA sequences from RAP-DB (<a href="https://rapdb.dna.affrc.go.jp/">https://rapdb.dna.affrc.go.jp/</a>) to supply as EST evidence, while the protein sequences from the 13 <i>Oryza</i> species project [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3086">37</a>] were used as protein evidence for the <i>MAKER</i> pipeline. Repetitive regions identified from the repeat analysis were used to mask out the repeat regions for this analysis. After a first round of running <i>MAKER</i>, the predicted genes were used by <i>SNAP</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 128" title="Korf I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics. 2004;5:59." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR128" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3099">128</a>] and <i>Augustus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 129" title="Stanke M, Diekhans M, Baertsch R, Haussler D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics. 2008;24:63744." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR129" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3105">129</a>] to create a training dataset of gene models, which was then used for a second round of <i>MAKER</i> gene annotation. Orthology between the genes from different rice genomes was determined with <i>Orthofinder</i> ver. 1.1.9 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Emms DM, Kelly S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 2015;16:157." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3115">59</a>]. Ortholog statuses were visualized with the <i>UpSetR</i> package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 130" title="Conway JR, Lex A, Gehlenborg N, Hancock J. UpSetR: an R package for the visualization of intersecting sets and their properties. Bioinformatics. 2017;33:293840." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR130" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3121">130</a>].</p><p>Gene ontology for the orthogroups that are missing specifically in the circum-basmati was examined by using the japonica Nipponbare gene and conducting a gene ontology enrichment analysis on <i>agriGO</i> v2.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 131" title="Tian T, Liu Y, Yan H, You Q, Yi X, Du Z, et al. agriGO v2.0: a GO analysis toolkit for the agricultural community, 2017 update. Nucleic Acids Res. 2017;45(Web Server issue):W1229." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR131" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3130">131</a>]. Gene ontology enrichment analysis for the circum-basmati-specific orthogroups was conducted first by predicting the function and gene ontology of each circum-basmati genome gene model using the eggnog pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 132" title="Huerta-Cepas J, Forslund K, Coelho LP, Szklarczyk D, Jensen LJ, von Mering C, et al. Fast genome-wide functional annotation through orthology assignment by eggNOG-Mapper. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:211522." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR132" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3133">132</a>]. We required an ontology to have more than 10 genes as a member for further consideration, and enrichment was tested through a hypergeometric test using the <i>GOstat</i> package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 133" title="Falcon S, Gentleman R. Using GOstats to test gene lists for GO term association. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:2578." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR133" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3139">133</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Repetitive DNA annotation</h3><p>The repeat content of each genome assembly was determined using <i>Repeatmasker</i> ver. 4.0.5 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RMDownload.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RMDownload.html</a>). We used the <i>Oryza</i>-specific repeat sequences that were identified from Choi et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Choi JY, Platts AE, Fuller DQ, Hsing Y-I, Wing RA, Purugganan MD. The rice paradox: multiple origins but single domestication in Asian rice. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:96979." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3160">14</a>] (DOI: <a href="https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.7cr0q">https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.7cr0q</a>), who had used <i>Repeatmodeler</i> ver. 1.0.8 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>) to de novo-annotate repetitive elements across wild and domesticated <i>Oryza</i> genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3178">37</a>].</p><p>LTR retrotransposons were annotated using the program <i>LTRharvest</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 134" title="Ellinghaus D, Kurtz S, Willhoeft U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC bioinformatics. 2008;9:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR134" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3187">134</a>] with parameters adapted from [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 135" title="Copetti D, Zhang J, El Baidouri M, Gao D, Wang J, Barghini E, et al. RiTE database: a resource database for genus-wide rice genomics and evolutionary biology. BMC Genomics. 2015;16:538." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR135" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3190">135</a>]. LTR retrotransposons were classified into superfamilies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Wicker T, Sabot F, Hua-Van A, Bennetzen JL, Capy P, Chalhoub B, et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat Rev Genet. 2007;8:97382." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3193">82</a>] using the program <i>RepeatClassifier</i> from the <i>RepeatModeler</i> suite. Annotated LTR retrotransposons were further classified into specific families using the 242 consensus sequences of LTR-RTs from the RetrOryza database [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Chaparro C, Guyot R, Zuccolo A, Pigu B, Panaud O. RetrOryza: a database of the rice LTR-retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35(Database issue):D6670." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3203">89</a>]. We used <i>blastn</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 136" title="Camacho C, Coulouris G, Avagyan V, Ma N, Papadopoulos J, Bealer K, et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics. 2009;10:421." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR136" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3209">136</a>] to search the RetrOryza sequences, and each of our candidate LTR retrotransposons was identified using the 80-80-80 rule [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Wicker T, Sabot F, Hua-Van A, Bennetzen JL, Capy P, Chalhoub B, et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat Rev Genet. 2007;8:97382." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3212">82</a>]: two TEs belong to the same family if they were 80% identical over at least 80bp and 80% of their length.</p><p>Insertion times for the LTR retrotransposons were estimated using the DNA divergence between pairs of LTR sequences [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="SanMiguel P, Gaut BS, Tikhonov A, Nakajima Y, Bennetzen JL. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat Genet. 1998;20:435." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3218">81</a>]. The L-INS-I algorithm in the alignment program <i>MAFFT</i> ver. 7.154b [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 137" title="Katoh K, Standley DM. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol Biol Evol. 2013;30:77280." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR137" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3224">137</a>] was used to align the LTR sequences. <i>PAML</i> ver. 4.8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 138" title="Yang Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol Biol Evol. 2007;24:158691." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR138" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3230">138</a>] was used to estimate the DNA divergence between the LTR sequences with the Kimura-2-parameter base substitution model [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 139" title="Kimura M. A simple method for estimating evolutionary rates of base substitutions through comparative studies of nucleotide sequences. J Mol Evol. 1980;16:11120." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR139" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3234">139</a>]. DNA divergence was converted to divergence time (i.e., time since the insertion of a LTR retrotransposon) approximating a base substitution rate of 1.310<sup>8</sup> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 140" title="Ma J, Bennetzen JL. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2004;101:1240410." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR140" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3239">140</a>], which is two times higher than the synonymous site substitution rate.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Presence/absence variation detection</h3><p>PAVs between the Nipponbare genome and the circum-basmati assemblies were detected using the <i>Assemblytics</i> suites [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Nattestad M, Schatz MC. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics. 2016;32:30213." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3253">60</a>]. Initially, the Nipponbare genome was used as the reference to align the circum-basmati assemblies using the program <i>Minimap2</i>. The resulting SAM files were converted to files in delta format using the <i>sam2delta.py</i> script from the <i>RaGOO</i> suite. The delta files were then uploaded onto the online <i>Assemblytics</i> analysis pipeline (<a href="http://assemblytics.com/">http://assemblytics.com/</a>). Repetitive regions would cause multiple regions in the Nipponbare or circum-basmati genomes to align to one another, and in that case, <i>Assemblytics</i> would call the same region as a PAV multiple times. Hence, any PAV regions that overlapped for at least 70% of their genomic coordinates were collapsed to a single region.</p><p>The combination of <i>ngmlr</i> and <i>sniffles</i> was also used to detect the PAVs that differed between the Nipponbare genome and the raw nanopore reads for the circum-basmati rice. Because <i>Assemblytics</i> only detects PAVs in the range of 50 to 100,000bp, we used this window as a size limit to filter out the PAVs called by <i>sniffles</i>. Only PAVs supported by more than 5 reads by <i>sniffles</i> were analyzed.</p><p><i>Assemblytics</i> and <i>sniffles</i> call the breakpoints of PAVs differently. <i>Assemblytics</i> calls a single-best breakpoint based on the genome alignment, while <i>sniffles</i> calls a breakpoint across a predicted interval. To find overlapping PAVs between <i>Assemblytics</i> and <i>sniffles</i>, we added 500bp upstream and downstream of the <i>Assemblytics</i>-predicted breakpoint positions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Detecting gene deletions across the circum<i>-</i>basmati population</h3><p>Genome-wide deletion frequencies of each gene were estimated using the 78-variety circum-basmati population genomic dataset. For each of the 78 varieties, raw sequencing reads were aligned to the circum-basmati and Nipponbare genomes using <i>bwa-mem</i>. Genome coverage per site was calculated using <i>bedtools genomecov</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 141" title="Quinlan AR, Hall IM. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics. 2010;26:8412." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR141" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3336">141</a>]. For each variety, the average read coverage was calculated for each gene, and a gene was designated as deleted if its average coverage was less than 0.05.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Whole-genome alignment of <i>Oryza</i> genomes assembled de novo</h3><p>Several genomes from published studies that were assembled de novo were analyzed. These include domesticated Asian rice genomes from the japonica variety group cv. Nipponbare [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature. 2005;436:793800." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3351">33</a>]; the indica variety group cvs. 93-11 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yu J, Hu S, Wang J, Wong GK-S, Li S, Liu B, et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science. 2002;296:7992." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3354">32</a>], IR8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3357">37</a>], IR64 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Schatz MC, Maron LG, Stein JC, Wences A, Gurtowski J, Biggers E, et al. Whole genome de novo assemblies of three divergent strains of rice, Oryza sativa , document novel gene space of aus and indica. Genome Biol. 2014;15:506." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3360">38</a>], MH63 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang J, Chen L-L, Xing F, Kudrna DA, Yao W, Copetti D, et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2016;113:E516371." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3363">40</a>], R498 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Du H, Yu Y, Ma Y, Gao Q, Cao Y, Chen Z, et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:15324." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3367">41</a>], and ZS97 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang J, Chen L-L, Xing F, Kudrna DA, Yao W, Copetti D, et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2016;113:E516371." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3370">40</a>]; the circum-aus variety group cvs. DJ123 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Schatz MC, Maron LG, Stein JC, Wences A, Gurtowski J, Biggers E, et al. Whole genome de novo assemblies of three divergent strains of rice, Oryza sativa , document novel gene space of aus and indica. Genome Biol. 2014;15:506." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3373">38</a>], Kasalath [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Sakai H, Kanamori H, Arai-Kichise Y, Shibata-Hatta M, Ebana K, Oono Y, et al. Construction of pseudomolecule sequences of the aus rice cultivar Kasalath for comparative genomics of Asian cultivated rice. DNA Res. 2014;21:397405." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3376">39</a>], and N22 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3379">37</a>]; and the circum-basmati variety group cv. GP295-1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhao Q, Feng Q, Lu H, Li Y, Wang A, Tian Q, et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat Genet. 2018;50:27884." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3382">42</a>]. Three genomes from wild rice species were also analyzed; these were <i>O. barthii</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang M, Yu Y, Haberer G, Marri PR, Fan C, Goicoechea JL, et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat Genet. 2014;46:9828." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3389">35</a>], <i>O. nivara</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3395">37</a>], and <i>O. rufipogon</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stein JC, Yu Y, Copetti D, Zwickl DJ, Zhang L, Zhang C, et al. Genomes of 13 domesticated and wild rice relatives highlight genetic conservation, turnover and innovation across the genus Oryza. Nat Genet. 2018;50:285." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3401">37</a>].</p><p>Alignment of the genomes assembled de novo was conducted using the approach outlined in Haudry et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 142" title="Haudry A, Platts AE, Vello E, Hoen DR, Leclercq M, Williamson RJ, et al. An atlas of over 90,000 conserved noncoding sequences provides insight into crucifer regulatory regions. Nat Genet. 2013;45:8918." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR142" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3407">142</a>], and this alignment approach has been used in another rice comparative genomic study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Choi JY, Platts AE, Fuller DQ, Hsing Y-I, Wing RA, Purugganan MD. The rice paradox: multiple origins but single domestication in Asian rice. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:96979." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3410">14</a>]. Briefly, this involved using the Nipponbare genome as the reference for aligning all other genome assemblies. Alignment between japonica and a query genome was conducted using <i>LASTZ</i> ver. 1.03.73 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 143" title="Harris RS. Improved pairwise alignment of genomic DNA. State College: PhD Thesis, The Pennsylvania State University; 2007." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR143" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3416">143</a>], and the alignment blocks were chained together using the UCSC Kent utilities [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 144" title="Kent WJ, Baertsch R, Hinrichs A, Miller W, Haussler D. Evolutions cauldron: duplication, deletion, and rearrangement in the mouse and human genomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2003;100:114849." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR144" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3419">144</a>]. For japonica genomic regions with multiple chains, the chain with the highest alignment score was chosen as the single-most orthologous region. This analyzes only one of the multiple regions that are potentially paralogous between the japonica and query genomes, but this was not expected to affect the downstream phylogenomic analysis of determining the origin and evolution of the circum<i>-</i>basmati rice variety group. All pairwise genome alignments between the japonica and query genomes were combined into a multi-genome alignment using <i>MULTIZ</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 145" title="Blanchette M, Kent WJ, Riemer C, Elnitski L, Smit AFA, Roskin KM, et al. Aligning multiple genomic sequences with the threaded blockset aligner. Genome Res. 2004;14:70815." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR145" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3429">145</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Phylogenomic analysis</h3><p>The multi-genome alignment was used to reconstruct the phylogenetic relationships between the domesticated and wild rice. Fourfold degenerate sites based on the gene model of the reference japonica genome were extracted using the <i>msa_view</i> program from the <i>phast</i> package ver. 1.4 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 146" title="Hubisz MJ, Pollard KS, Siepel A. PHAST and RPHAST: phylogenetic analysis with space/time models. Brief Bioinformatics. 2011;12:4151." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR146" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3447">146</a>]. The fourfold degenerate sites were used by <i>RAxML</i> ver. 8.2.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 147" title="Stamatakis A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:13123." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR147" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3453">147</a>] to build a maximum likelihood-based tree, using a general time-reversible DNA substitution model with gamma-distributed rate variation.</p><p>To investigate the genome-wide landscape of introgression and incomplete lineage sorting, we examined the phylogenetic topologies of each gene [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 148" title="Martin SH, Jiggins CD. Interpreting the genomic landscape of introgression. Curr Opin Genet Dev. 2017;47:6974." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR148" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3459">148</a>]. For a three-species phylogeny using <i>O. barthii</i> as an outgroup, there are three possible topologies. For each gene, topology-testing methods [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 149" title="Goldman N, Anderson JP, Rodrigo AG, Olmstead R. Likelihood-based tests of topologies in phylogenetics. Syst Biol. 2000;49:65270." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR149" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3465">149</a>] can be used to determine which topology significantly fits the gene of interest [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Choi JY, Platts AE, Fuller DQ, Hsing Y-I, Wing RA, Purugganan MD. The rice paradox: multiple origins but single domestication in Asian rice. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:96979." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3468">14</a>]. <i>RAxML</i>-estimated site-likelihood values were calculated for each gene and the significant topology was determined using the Approximately Unbiased (AU) test [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Shimodaira H. An approximately unbiased test of phylogenetic tree selection. Syst Biol. 2002;51:492508." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3475">92</a>] from the program <i>CONSEL</i> v. 0.20 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 150" title="Shimodaira H, Hasegawa M. CONSEL: for assessing the confidence of phylogenetic tree selection. Bioinformatics. 2001;17:12467." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR150" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3481">150</a>]. Genes with AU test results with a likelihood difference of 0 were omitted, and the topology with an AU test support of greater than 0.95 was selected.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Testing for evidence of admixture</h3><p>Evidence of admixture between variety groups was detected using the ABBA-BABA test D-statistics [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Green RE, Krause J, Briggs AW, Maricic T, Stenzel U, Kircher M, et al. A draft sequence of the Neandertal genome. Science. 2010;328:71022." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3492">93</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Durand EY, Patterson N, Reich D, Slatkin M. Testing for ancient admixture between closely related populations. Mol Biol Evol. 2011;28:223952." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3495">94</a>]. In a rooted three-taxon phylogeny [i.e., ((P1,P2),P3),O where P1, P2, and P3 are the variety groups of interest and O is outgroup <i>O. barthii</i>], admixture can be inferred from the combination of ancestral (A) and derived (B) allelic states of each individual. The ABBA conformation arises when variety groups P2 and P3 share derived alleles, while the BABA conformation is found when P1 and P3 share derived alleles. The difference in the frequency of the ABBA and BABA conformations is measured by the D-statistics, where significantly positive D-statistics indicate admixture between the P2 and P3 variety groups, and significantly negative D-statistics indicate admixture between the P1 and P3 variety groups. The genome was divided into 100,000-bp bins for jackknife resampling and calculation of the standard errors. The significance of the D-statistics was calculated using the <i>Z</i>-test, and D-statistics with <i>z</i>-scores greater than |3.9| (<i>p</i>&lt;0.0001) were considered significant.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Population genomic analysis</h3><p>We downloaded FASTQ files from the 3K Rice Genome Project [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557:439." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3519">7</a>] for rice varieties that were determined to be circum-basmati varieties in that project. An additional 8 circum-basmati varieties were sequenced on the Illumina sequencing platform as part of this study. The raw reads were aligned to the scaffolded Basmati 334 genome using the program <i>bwa-mem</i>. PCR duplicates were determined computationally and removed using the program <i>picard</i> version 2.9.0 (<a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>). Genotype calls for each site were conducted using the <i>GATK HaplotypeCaller</i> engine using the option -ERC GVCF. The output files were in the genomic variant call format (gVCF), and the gVCFs from each variety were merged using the <i>GATK GenotypeGVCFs</i> engine.</p><p>SNP and INDEL variants from the population variant file were filtered independently using the <i>GATK</i> bestpractice hard filter pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 151" title="Van der Auwera GA, Carneiro MO, Hartl C, Poplin R, del Angel G, Levy-Moonshine A, et al. From FastQ data to high-confidence variant calls: the genome analysis toolkit best practices pipeline. In: Current protocols in bioinformatics. Hoboken: Wiley; 2013. p. 11.10.111.10.33. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1002/0471250953.bi1110s43&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR151" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3545">151</a>]. SNP variants within 5bps of an INDEL variant were filtered. <i>Vcftools</i> version 0.1.15 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 152" title="Danecek P, Auton A, Abecasis G, Albers CA, Banks E, DePristo MA, et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:21568." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR152" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3551">152</a>] was used to filter sites for which genotypes were not called for more than 20% of the varieties. Because domesticated rice is an inbreeding species, we also implemented a heterozygosity filter by filtering out sites that had a heterozygote genotype in more than 5% of the samples using the program <i>vcffilterjdk.jar</i> from the <i>jvarkit</i> suite (<a href="https://figshare.com/articles/JVarkit_java_based_utilities_for_Bioinformatics/1425030">https://figshare.com/articles/JVarkit_java_based_utilities_for_Bioinformatics/1425030</a>). Missing genotypes were imputed and phased using <i>Beagle</i> version 4.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 153" title="Browning BL, Browning SR. Genotype imputation with millions of reference samples. Am J Hum Genet. 2016;98:11626." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR153" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3568">153</a>].</p><p>To examine the within-circum-basmati variety group population structure, we first randomly pruned the sites by sampling a polymorphic site every 200,000bp using <i>plink</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 154" title="Purcell S, Neale B, Todd-Brown K, Thomas L, Ferreira MAR, Bender D, et al. PLINK: a tool set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am J Hum Genet. 2007;81:55975." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR154" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3577">154</a>]. <i>Plink</i> was also used to conduct a principal component analysis. Ancestry proportions of each sample were estimated using <i>fastSTRUCTURE</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Raj A, Stephens M, Pritchard JK. fastSTRUCTURE: variational inference of population structure in large SNP data sets. Genetics. 2014;197:57389." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3586">97</a>]. A neighbor-joining tree was built by calculating the pairwise genetic distances between samples using the Kronecker delta function-based equation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 155" title="Freedman AH, Gronau I, Schweizer RM, Ortega-Del Vecchyo D, Han E, Silva PM, et al. Genome sequencing highlights the dynamic early history of dogs. PLoS Genet. 2014;10:e1004016." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR155" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3590">155</a>]. From the genetic distance matrix, a neighbor-joining tree was built using the program <i>FastME</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 156" title="Lefort V, Desper R, Gascuel O. FastME 2.0: a comprehensive, accurate, and fast distance-based phylogeny inference program. Mol Biol Evol. 2015;32:2798800." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR156" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3596">156</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Evolutionary relationships among the circum<i>-</i>basmati, circum<i>-</i>aus, and japonica populations</h3><p>To investigate the evolutionary origins of the circum-basmati population, we focused on the landrace varieties that had been sequenced with a genome-wide coverage of greater than 14. The population data for the circum-aus and japonica populations were obtained from the 3K Rice Genome Project [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557:439." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3614">7</a>], from which we also analyzed only the landrace varieties that had been sequenced with a genome-wide coverage greater than 14. For an outgroup, we obtained <i>O. barthii</i> sequencing data from previous studies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang M, Yu Y, Haberer G, Marri PR, Fan C, Goicoechea JL, et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat Genet. 2014;46:9828." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3620">35</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Choi JY, Zaidem M, Gutaker R, Dorph K, Singh RK, Purugganan MD. The complex geography of domestication of the African rice Oryza glaberrima. PLoS Genet. 2019;15:e1007414." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3623">74</a>] and focused on the samples that were not likely to be feralized rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Choi JY, Zaidem M, Gutaker R, Dorph K, Singh RK, Purugganan MD. The complex geography of domestication of the African rice Oryza glaberrima. PLoS Genet. 2019;15:e1007414." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3626">74</a>]. The Illumina reads were aligned to the scaffolded Basmati 334 genome and SNPs were called and filtered according to the procedure outlined in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Sec29">Population genomic analysis</a> section.</p><p>We examined the genome-wide local topological relationship using <i>twisst</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Martin SH, Van Belleghem SM. Exploring evolutionary relationships across the genome using topology weighting. Genetics. 2017;206:42938." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3639">95</a>]. Initially, a sliding window analysis was conducted to estimate the local phylogenetic trees in windows with a size of 100 or 500 polymorphic sites using <i>RAxML</i> with the GTRCAT substitution model. The script <i>raxml_sliding_windows.py</i> from the <i>genomics_general</i> package by Simon Martin (<a href="https://github.com/simonhmartin/genomics_general/tree/master/phylo">https://github.com/simonhmartin/genomics_general/tree/master/phylo</a>) was used. The complete option of <i>twisst</i> was used to calculate the exact weighting of each local window.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec31">Treemix analysis</h3><p>A past study by Wang et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Wang H, Vieira FG, Crawford JE, Chu C, Nielsen R. Asian wild rice is a hybrid swarm with extensive gene flow and feralization from domesticated rice. Genome Res. 2017;27:102938." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3667">90</a>] had found evidence of admixture between domesticated rice and wild rice in a dataset of genome sequences from a global sample of rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Huang X, Kurata N, Wei X, Wang Z-X, Wang A, Zhao Q, et al. A map of rice genome variation reveals the origin of cultivated rice. Nature. 2012;490:497501." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3670">16</a>]. Therefore, we obtained <i>O. rufipogon</i> genome data from a separate study that sequenced five samples to a high coverage (&gt;10) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 157" title="Xu X, Liu X, Ge S, Jensen JD, Hu F, Li X, et al. Resequencing 50 accessions of cultivated and wild rice yields markers for identifying agronomically important genes. Nat Biotechnol. 2012;30:10511." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR157" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3676">157</a>]. The <i>O. rufipogon</i> population sample was combined with the population genomic dataset from the section <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Sec30">Evolutionary relationships among the circum<i>-</i>basmati, circum<i>-</i>aus, and japonica populations</a>. Polymorphic sites were randomly selected every 200kbp, and this dataset was used by Treemix version 1.13 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 158" title="Pickrell JK, Pritchard JK. Inference of population splits and mixtures from genome-wide allele frequency data. PLoS Genet. 2012;8:e1002967." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR158" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3692">158</a>] to fit migration edges on a bifurcating tree.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec32"><i>ai</i> demographic model</h3><p>The demography model underlying the evolution of circum-basmati rice was tested using the diffusion approximation method of <i>ai</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Gutenkunst RN, Hernandez RD, Williamson SH, Bustamante CD. Inferring the joint demographic history of multiple populations from multidimensional SNP frequency data. PLoS Genet. 2009;5:e1000695." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3709">96</a>]. A visual representation of the 13 demographic models that were examined can be seen in Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S7. The population group and genotype calls used in the twisst analysis were also used to calculate the site allele frequencies. To conduct a <i>ai</i> analysis for three populations with polarized allele frequency spectrum, the polymorphic sites were polarized using the <i>O. barthii</i> reference genome. Using the Basmati 334 reference genome, the <i>O. barthii</i> genome was aligned using the same procedure outlined in the section <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#Sec26">Whole-genome alignment of <i>Oryza</i> genomes assembled de novo</a><i>.</i> This genome alignment was then used to determine the outgroup sequence status for every polymorphic site.</p><p>We optimized the model parameter estimates using the Nelder-Mead method and randomly perturbed the parameter values for four rounds. Parameter values were perturbed for threefold, twofold, twofold, and onefold in each subsequent round, while the perturbation was conducted for 10, 20, 30, and 40 replicates in each subsequent round. In each round, parameter values from the best likelihood model of the previous round were used as the starting parameter values for the next round. Parameter values from the round with the highest likelihood were chosen to parameterize each demographic model. Akaike Information Criteria (AIC) values were used to compare demography models. The demography model with the lowest AIC was chosen as the best-fitting model.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec33">Agronomic trait measurements</h3><p>Data on geolocation of collection as well as on seed dimensions and seed weight for each of the circum-basmati landrace varieties included in this study were obtained from passport data included in the online platform Genesys (<a href="https://www.genesys-pgr.org/welcome">https://www.genesys-pgr.org/welcome</a>).</p></div></div></section>
                    <section aria-labelledby="availability-of-data-and-materials" data-title="Availability of data and materials"><div class="c-article-section" id="availability-of-data-and-materials-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="availability-of-data-and-materials">Availability of data and materials</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="availability-of-data-and-materials-content">
              
              <p>Raw nanopore sequencing FAST5 files generated from this study are available at the European Nucleotide Archive under bioproject ID PRJEB28274 (ERX3327648-ERX3327652) for Basmati 334 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 159" title="Choi JY, Zye ZN, Groen SC, Dai X, Rughani P, Zaaijer S, et al. (2019). &#xA;https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/browser/view/PRJEB28274&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR159" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3825">159</a>] and PRJEB32431 (ERX3334790-ERX3334793) for Dom Sufid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 160" title="Choi JY, Zye ZN, Groen SC, Dai X, Rughani P, Zaaijer S, et al. Nanopore sequencing and assembly of Sadri rice. European Nucleotide Archive. (2019). &#xA;https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/browser/view/PRJEB32431&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR160" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3828">160</a>]. Associated FASTQ files are available under ERX3498039-ERX3498043 for Basmati 334 and ERX3498024-ERX3498027 for Dom Sufid. Illumina sequencing generated from this study can be found under bioproject ID PRJNA422249 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 161" title="Choi JY, Zye ZN, Groen SC, Dai X, Rughani P, Zaaijer S, et al. Whole genome re-sequencing of a mini-core set of Oryza sativa landraces for studies of salinity tolerance in rice. European Nucleotide Archive. (2019). &#xA;https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/browser/view/PRJNA422249&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR161" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3831">161</a>] and PRJNA557122 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 162" title="Choi JY, Zye ZN, Groen SC, Dai X, Rughani P, Zaaijer S, et al. Illumina sequencing of Oryza sativa subpopulation/variety. European Nucleotide Archive. (2019). &#xA;https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/browser/view/PRJNA557122&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR162" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3834">162</a>]. A genome browser for both genome assemblies can be found at <a href="http://purugganan-genomebrowser.bio.nyu.edu/cgi-bin/hgTracks?db=Basmati334">http://purugganan-genomebrowser.bio.nyu.edu/cgi-bin/hgTracks?db=Basmati334</a> for Basmati 334 and <a href="http://purugganan-genomebrowser.bio.nyu.edu/cgi-bin/hgTracks?db=DomSufid">http://purugganan-genomebrowser.bio.nyu.edu/cgi-bin/hgTracks?db=DomSufid</a> for Dom Sufid. All data including the assembly, annotation, genome alignment, and population VCFs generated from this study can be found at Zenodo (<a href="https://doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.3355330">https://doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.3355330</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 163" title="Choi JY, Zye ZN, Groen SC, Dai X, Rughani P, Zaaijer S, et al. Dataset for Nanopore-based genome assembly and the evolutionary genomics of basmati rice. Zenodo. 2019; &#xA;https://doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.3355330&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-020-1938-2#ref-CR163" id="ref-link-section-d14746e3850">163</a>].</p>

Archaeol Anthropol Sci. 2010;2:11531.</p><p class="c-article-references__links u-hide-print"><a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2Fs12520-010-0035-y" aria-label="View reference 5">Article</a>
